\maketitle
\tableofcontents

\chapter{Introduction to Generative AI: A Historical Perspective}

\section{Introduction}

Generative Artificial Intelligence represents one of the most transformative
technological developments of the twenty-first century. Unlike traditional AI
systems that analyze and classify existing data, generative models create new
content --- text, images, code, music, and more --- that can be fluent,
coherent, and sometimes indistinguishable from human-produced work. To
understand how we arrived at this capability, we must trace the evolution of
artificial intelligence from its theoretical foundations in the 1940s to the
transformer architectures that power modern large language models.

This chapter follows the key research milestones that paved the way for
today's generative AI systems. Each breakthrough built upon previous work,
gradually expanding our understanding of how machines can learn, represent
knowledge, and ultimately generate novel content. From the earliest
mathematical models of neurons to the attention mechanisms that revolutionized
natural language processing, this historical journey reveals both the
persistence of fundamental ideas and the explosive impact of recent
innovations.

Several foundational equations appear in this chapter. If you are not
mathematically inclined, feel free to skim them; each equation is accompanied
by a figure and plain-language explanation, and we develop the underlying
mathematics in detail in the chapters that follow.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{images/ai_timeline.png}
    \caption{Key milestones in the history of neural networks and generative
    AI, from the McCulloch-Pitts neuron (1943) to the transformer revolution
    (2017) and the Nobel Prize recognition of the field (2024). Each milestone
    is discussed in this chapter.}
    \label{fig:ai_timeline}
\end{figure}

\section{The Foundations: Early Neural Network Theory}

\subsection{The McCulloch-Pitts Neuron (1943)}
\label{sec:mcp}

The conceptual journey toward generative AI began in 1943 with Warren
McCulloch and Walter Pitts' seminal paper~\cite{mcculloch1943logical},
``A Logical Calculus of the Ideas Immanent in Nervous Activity.'' This work
established the first mathematical model of a biological neuron, laying the
theoretical groundwork for all neural networks that would follow.

\textbf{The researchers behind the paper} were as unlikely a pair as the
field has produced. Warren McCulloch was a gregarious, bearded psychiatrist
and neuroscientist at the University of Illinois at Chicago, already in his forties and
well established in academic circles. He had spent years trying to express the
logic of neural activity in mathematical terms but lacked the formal tools to
do so. Walter Pitts was seventeen years old, a self-taught mathematical
prodigy who had run away from an abusive home in Detroit, taught himself
Greek and Latin to read Bertrand Russell in the original, and written to
Russell directly to point out errors in \textit{Principia Mathematica} ---
receiving a serious reply. He had no formal academic affiliation, no degree,
and no permanent address; McCulloch found him essentially living on the
streets of Chicago and invited him into his home and laboratory. Their
collaboration became legendary in the early cybernetics community, and Pitts
was considered by many of their contemporaries --- including Norbert Wiener
and John von Neumann --- to be among the most brilliant logicians alive.
The paper they produced together changed the course of computing.

McCulloch and Pitts proposed that neurons could be modeled as binary
threshold units: each artificial neuron receives multiple inputs, computes a
weighted sum, and produces a binary output based on whether that sum exceeds
a threshold.

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:mcp}
y = \begin{cases}
1 & \text{if } \displaystyle\sum_{i=1}^{n} w_i x_i \geq \theta \\[6pt]
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\end{equation}

where $x_i$ are the input signals, $w_i$ are the synaptic weights, and
$\theta$ is the threshold value. Figure~\ref{fig:mcp_neuron} shows this
mechanism visually.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{images/mcp_neuron.png}
    \caption{The McCulloch-Pitts neuron. Multiple binary inputs $x_1, \ldots,
    x_n$ are multiplied by weights $w_1, \ldots, w_n$ and summed. If the
    weighted sum meets or exceeds the threshold $\theta$, the neuron fires
    (output 1); otherwise it is silent (output 0). The weights were fixed by
    hand in the original model --- the crucial innovation of \emph{learning}
    the weights would come fifteen years later with the perceptron.}
    \label{fig:mcp_neuron}
\end{figure}

The profound insight of this work was demonstrating that networks of such
simplified neurons could, in principle, compute any logical function.
McCulloch and Pitts showed that these networks were Turing complete --- they
could perform any computation that a digital computer could perform. This
established a crucial link between neural biology, logic, and computation that
would inspire researchers for decades.

The model had important limitations: weights were set by hand rather than
learned, and the hard threshold produced binary outputs rather than graded
responses. Despite these constraints, it proved that neural-like computation
was theoretically viable and planted the seed for adaptive learning systems.

Tragically, Walter Pitts's story does not end triumphantly. In the late
1950s, a personal falling-out with Norbert Wiener --- rooted partly in
interpersonal conflicts and partly in the politics of the early cybernetics
movement --- left Pitts isolated and deeply depressed. He withdrew from
research, began drinking heavily, and burned much of his unpublished work.
He died in 1969 at the age of 46, largely forgotten by the mainstream
scientific community, on the same year that Minsky and Papert published
their famous critique of the very field he had helped to create. The
foundational paper he co-authored at seventeen remained one of the most cited
in the history of neuroscience.

\subsection{The Perceptron: Learning Emerges (1958)}
\label{sec:perceptron}

Fifteen years after the McCulloch-Pitts neuron, Frank Rosenblatt introduced
a revolutionary advance: a neural network that could \emph{learn} from
examples. His 1958 paper~\cite{rosenblatt1958perceptron}, ``The Perceptron:
A Probabilistic Model for Information Storage and Organization in the Brain,''
described both a theoretical model and a physical machine capable of pattern
recognition.

The perceptron built upon the McCulloch-Pitts neuron but added a critical
innovation --- a learning algorithm that adjusted weights based on training
examples, gradually improving its ability to classify patterns:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:perceptron}
w_i^{(new)} = w_i^{(old)} + \eta\, (t - y)\, x_i
\end{equation}

where $\eta$ is the learning rate, $t$ is the correct target output, and
$y$ is the actual output. This rule embodies a principle that persists in
modern deep learning: when the model makes a mistake, weights are adjusted in
proportion to the error, moving the decision boundary toward correct
classification. Figure~\ref{fig:perceptron_learning} illustrates how this
boundary shifts through training.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/perceptron_learning.png}
    \caption{The perceptron learning rule in action. Left: before training,
    the decision boundary (dashed line) misclassifies several examples.
    Right: after applying the weight update rule across multiple examples,
    the boundary has shifted to correctly separate the two classes. The
    perceptron convergence theorem guarantees this process terminates for
    any linearly separable dataset.}
    \label{fig:perceptron_learning}
\end{figure}

Rosenblatt's physical implementation, the Mark I Perceptron (1958), was
built at Cornell and could recognize simple geometric shapes using 400
photocells connected to randomly wired units with adjustable potentiometers.
The perceptron learning theorem provided a mathematical guarantee: for any
linearly separable problem, the algorithm converges to a correct solution in
finite time. This theoretical confidence attracted wide interest and
significant optimism.

\subsection{The First AI Winter: Perceptrons Criticized (1969)}
\label{sec:ai_winter}

The optimism surrounding perceptrons faced a severe challenge in 1969 when
Marvin Minsky and Seymour Papert published ``Perceptrons''~\cite{minsky1969perceptrons}.
Through rigorous mathematical analysis, they demonstrated that a single-layer
perceptron cannot solve the XOR problem --- a simple logical function where
the output is true only when the inputs differ. XOR is not linearly separable:
no single straight line can divide the positive and negative examples in the
input space.

More broadly, Minsky and Papert showed that single-layer perceptrons could
only classify patterns that are linearly separable, excluding many real-world
problems. While they acknowledged that multi-layer networks might overcome
these limitations, they were pessimistic about the prospects of finding
effective training methods for such networks.

The critique was technically correct, but it was also incomplete: the
limitations applied only to \emph{single-layer} networks, and the problem
would prove solvable once researchers discovered how to train deeper
architectures. But that discovery was still seventeen years away. Funding
for neural network research dried up, ushering in the first ``AI Winter'' ---
a period of reduced interest and investment that lasted through the 1970s.

\section{The Renaissance: Backpropagation and Deep Learning}

\subsection{Backpropagation: Training Deep Networks (1986)}
\label{sec:backprop}

The neural network renaissance began in earnest with the 1986 paper
\cite{rumelhart1986learning} ``Learning Representations by Back-propagating
Errors'' by David Rumelhart, Geoffrey Hinton, and Ronald Williams.
Backpropagation solved the problem Minsky and Papert had identified: how to
train neural networks with multiple layers.

The algorithm extends gradient descent to networks with hidden layers by
computing, for each weight, how much that weight contributed to the overall
error. The key insight is the chain rule of calculus (developed in detail in
Chapter~2): because the output of each layer feeds into the next, the error
signal can be decomposed into a product of local gradients and propagated
backward through the network, layer by layer:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:backprop}
\frac{\partial E}{\partial w_{ij}}
    = \frac{\partial E}{\partial y_j}
      \cdot \frac{\partial y_j}{\partial \text{net}_j}
      \cdot \frac{\partial \text{net}_j}{\partial w_{ij}}
\end{equation}

where $E$ is the total error, $y_j$ is the output of neuron $j$,
$\text{net}_j$ is its weighted input sum, and $w_{ij}$ is the weight
connecting neuron $i$ to neuron $j$. Each factor in this product is a
local quantity that can be computed from information already available at
that layer. Figure~\ref{fig:backprop} shows how the error signal flows
backward through a network.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/backprop.png}
    \caption{Backpropagation in a three-layer network. During the forward
    pass (left to right), inputs produce a prediction. During the backward
    pass (right to left), the difference between the prediction and the
    correct answer --- the error $E$ --- is propagated back through each
    layer. Each weight receives a gradient signal telling it how much to
    change, computed via the chain rule. Layers closer to the output receive
    the signal first; layers closer to the input receive a progressively
    transformed version of it.}
    \label{fig:backprop}
\end{figure}

Rumelhart, Hinton, and Williams demonstrated that networks trained with
backpropagation could learn internal representations --- patterns in the
hidden layers that were not explicitly taught but emerged from the training
process. These learned representations could capture hierarchical features,
with early layers learning simple patterns and deeper layers combining them
into complex concepts.

The impact was immediate. Backpropagation showed that multi-layer networks
could solve XOR and other non-linearly separable problems. It enabled neural
networks to tackle real-world tasks in speech recognition and pattern
classification, and it overcame the theoretical barrier that Minsky and
Papert's critique had erected. The approach of learning hierarchical
representations through gradient descent remains the foundation of deep
learning today.

\textbf{Nobel Prize recognition.} In October 2024, the Nobel Prize in Physics
was awarded to John Hopfield and Geoffrey Hinton for foundational work on
neural networks that enabled machine learning. Hopfield, a physicist at
Princeton, had in 1982 described a neural network model --- now known as the
Hopfield network~\cite{hopfield1982neural} --- that could store and retrieve
memories by minimizing an energy function, drawing a direct analogy between
neural networks and the physics of spin glasses. Hinton, who appears in this
chapter as a co-inventor of backpropagation, extended this line of work with
the Boltzmann machine and later became one of the most influential figures in
modern deep learning. The Nobel Committee cited their work as providing
``the physical tools that helped build powerful machine learning.'' The award
to a physicist and a computer scientist for work on neural networks reflects
how deeply these ideas have penetrated across scientific disciplines, and it
represents a formal recognition that the mathematical and physical principles
underlying modern AI have genuine scientific significance --- not merely
engineering utility.

\section{The Modern Era: Deep Learning Revolution}

\subsection{Convolutional Networks and the ImageNet Moment (2012)}
\label{sec:imagenet}

Despite backpropagation's theoretical power, neural networks struggled to
compete with other machine learning methods through the 1990s and 2000s.
Networks were difficult to train, prone to memorizing training data, and
often outperformed by simpler statistical methods. This changed dramatically
in 2012.

The paper~\cite{krizhevsky2012imagenet} ``ImageNet Classification with Deep
Convolutional Neural Networks'' by Alex Krizhevsky, Ilya Sutskever, and
Geoffrey Hinton presented a deep convolutional neural network that won the
ImageNet image recognition challenge by a margin that shocked the field:
where the previous year's winner had achieved around 26\% error, this
network achieved 16\%. The gap was large enough that it immediately
redirected the attention of the entire computer vision community.

The architecture combined three key ideas. First, \emph{convolutional layers}
apply small learned filters across the image --- rather than connecting every
neuron to every pixel --- capturing local patterns such as edges, textures,
and shapes while dramatically reducing the number of parameters. A
convolutional layer applies a filter $g$ of size $k \times k$ to a local
patch of the input $f$:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:conv}
(f * g)[m, n] = \sum_{i=0}^{k-1} \sum_{j=0}^{k-1} f[m+i,\; n+j] \cdot g[i, j]
\end{equation}

where $[m, n]$ is the position of the filter's top-left corner on the input.
Figure~\ref{fig:convolution} illustrates a filter sliding across an image.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/convolution.png}
    \caption{A $3 \times 3$ convolutional filter sliding across an input image
    patch. At each position, the filter values are multiplied elementwise with
    the corresponding input values and summed to produce one output value. The
    same filter is applied at every position, allowing the network to detect
    the same pattern (for example, a horizontal edge) anywhere in the image
    using a single set of learnable weights.}
    \label{fig:convolution}
\end{figure}

Second, \emph{ReLU activations} --- $f(x) = \max(0, x)$ --- replaced
traditional sigmoid functions, accelerating training by avoiding vanishing
gradients. Third, training on \emph{GPUs} made it feasible to process
millions of images in reasonable time, demonstrating that scale and
computation were as important as algorithmic innovation.

The deeper lesson of ImageNet was that deep networks could learn
\emph{hierarchical representations}: early layers detect edges and colors,
middle layers detect textures and shapes, and late layers detect object
parts and categories. This hierarchical structure proved to be as important
for generation as for recognition --- to generate a realistic image of a
cat, a model must understand what a cat is at multiple levels of abstraction.

\subsection{From Sequential to Parallel: The Problem That Transformers Solved}
\label{sec:rnn_limitation}

By the mid-2010s, the dominant approach to processing sequences --- text,
speech, time series --- was the \emph{recurrent neural network} (RNN) and
its more capable variant, the Long Short-Term Memory network (LSTM). These
models processed a sequence one element at a time, maintaining a hidden
state that carried information forward from earlier positions:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:rnn}
h_t = f(W_h\, h_{t-1} + W_x\, x_t)
\end{equation}

where $h_t$ is the hidden state at step $t$, $x_t$ is the input at step
$t$, and $W_h$, $W_x$ are learned weight matrices. Figure~\ref{fig:rnn_vs_transformer}
contrasts this sequential computation with the parallel approach of the
Transformer.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/rnn_vs_transformer.png}
    \caption{Sequential versus parallel sequence processing. Left: a
    recurrent network processes tokens one at a time, passing a hidden state
    from each step to the next. Information from early tokens must travel
    through many intermediate steps to influence later ones, creating a
    bottleneck for long sequences and making training slow because steps
    cannot be parallelized. Right: a Transformer processes all tokens
    simultaneously, with each token attending directly to every other token.
    There is no intermediate chain --- any two positions are one step apart.}
    \label{fig:rnn_vs_transformer}
\end{figure}

The sequential nature of RNNs created two serious problems. First, training
could not be parallelized across the sequence, making it slow on modern
hardware. Second, information from early in the sequence had to travel
through many intermediate hidden states to reach later positions, making it
difficult for the model to reliably connect information across long distances ---
even with LSTMs' gating mechanisms designed to address this. These were
fundamental architectural constraints, not implementation issues.

\subsection{Attention Is All You Need: The Transformer (2017)}
\label{sec:transformer_history}

The solution came in 2017 with ``Attention Is All You Need''~\cite{vaswani2017attention}
by Vaswani and colleagues at Google. The Transformer abandoned recurrent
processing entirely in favor of \emph{self-attention}: a mechanism that
allows every position in a sequence to attend directly to every other
position in a single parallel operation.

For each element in the sequence, the mechanism computes three vectors:
a Query ($Q$, what am I looking for?), a Key ($K$, what do I offer?), and
a Value ($V$, what information do I contain?). The attention output is a
weighted sum of values, where the weight between two positions is determined
by how well their query and key vectors match:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:attention_ch1}
\text{Attention}(Q, K, V)
    = \text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right) V
\end{equation}

where $d_k$ is the dimension of the key vectors and the $\sqrt{d_k}$ scaling
prevents the dot products from growing too large. Figure~\ref{fig:attention_ch1}
illustrates this mechanism.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/attention_ch1.png}
    \caption{Scaled dot-product attention. Each token projects itself into
    three roles simultaneously: Query (what it is looking for), Key (what
    it offers to others), and Value (the information it will share).
    Attention scores are computed as dot products between queries and keys,
    scaled and passed through softmax to produce weights that sum to one.
    The output for each token is a weighted combination of all value vectors,
    concentrating on the most relevant positions. All of this computation
    occurs in parallel across the entire sequence.}
    \label{fig:attention_ch1}
\end{figure}

The Transformer's advantages over recurrent networks were immediate and
decisive. Because all attention computations happen in parallel, training
could exploit modern GPU hardware far more effectively than sequential
processing. Because any two positions communicate directly through the
attention mechanism, there is no degradation over distance --- a word at
position 1 and a word at position 500 are equally accessible to each other.
The architecture also proved remarkably scalable: performance improved
reliably as models grew larger and were trained on more data, a property
we explore in depth in Chapter~4.

\section{From Transformers to Generative AI}
\label{sec:transformers_to_genai}

The Transformer was introduced for machine translation, but researchers
quickly recognized that its properties made it ideal for a much broader
task: learning to generate content by modeling the probability distribution
over sequences. Three conceptual developments, each enabled by the
Transformer, produced the generative AI systems we see today.

\textbf{Pretraining at scale.} The Transformer's parallelism made it
practical to train on datasets of unprecedented size. Researchers found
that as models grew larger and training data grew larger, performance
improved predictably --- following \emph{scaling laws} that showed loss
decreasing as a power function of model size and data volume. This meant
that investing in scale produced reliable returns, incentivizing a
sustained increase in model size from millions of parameters in the late
2010s to hundreds of billions by the early 2020s. Crucially, a model
pretrained on a large general corpus could be adapted to specific tasks
with far less data than would be required to train from scratch --- the
knowledge learned during pretraining transferred.

\textbf{Unified token representation across modalities.} Text, images,
audio, and other signals can all be represented as sequences of discrete
tokens. Text is tokenized into subword units; images are divided into
patches and encoded as visual tokens; audio waveforms can be discretized
into acoustic tokens. Once all modalities are expressed as token sequences,
the same Transformer architecture and the same training objective ---
predict the next token --- applies uniformly across them. This unification
enabled a single class of model to span text generation, image synthesis,
code completion, and scientific prediction within a common framework.

\textbf{From task-specific to general-purpose models.} Earlier machine
learning systems were designed for specific tasks: one model for translation,
another for classification, another for question answering. Transformer-based
models trained on sufficiently large and diverse data learned general
capabilities that transferred across tasks without task-specific
architectures. The same model weights could translate, summarize,
answer questions, write code, and generate images by virtue of having
internalized broad statistical regularities of the training data.
Conditioning on a prompt --- as described in Chapter~3 --- allowed users
to direct these general capabilities toward specific tasks without
retraining the model.

Together, these three developments --- scale, unified tokenization, and
generality --- transformed the Transformer from a translation system into the
foundation for modern generative AI. The mathematical and conceptual tools
that make this possible are the subject of the remaining chapters.

\section{Conclusion}

The history traced in this chapter spans eight decades of research,
setbacks, and breakthroughs. Several themes stand out.

\textbf{Ideas persist.} The weighted sum of inputs that McCulloch and Pitts
wrote down in 1943 appears, in more sophisticated form, in every transformer
layer operating today. The gradient-based learning rule that Rosenblatt
proposed in 1958 is the foundation of backpropagation and modern optimizers.
Fundamental mathematical structures have proven more durable than any
particular implementation.

\textbf{Limitations drive progress.} The AI Winter was not a failure but a
clarification: Minsky and Papert showed precisely what single-layer networks
could not do, which pointed directly at what needed to be solved. The
sequential bottleneck of recurrent networks defined the problem that the
Transformer solved. Constraints have repeatedly been the most productive
direction-setters in the field.

\textbf{Scale amplifies.} At every stage, increases in scale --- data,
computation, model size --- have unlocked capabilities that seemed out of
reach at smaller scales. The deep learning revolution of 2012 required
GPUs and millions of labeled images. The generative AI revolution of the
2020s required transformer architectures and trillions of tokens. In both
cases, the right architecture combined with sufficient scale produced
emergent capabilities.

\textbf{The field is now recognized as foundational science.} The 2024
Nobel Prize in Physics awarded to Hopfield and Hinton marks a formal
acknowledgment that neural network research has produced ideas of genuine
scientific depth, not merely engineering utility.

Chapter~2 develops the mathematical foundations --- linear algebra,
probability, and calculus --- that underlie everything described here.
Chapter~3 formalizes what it means to generate: probability distributions,
conditioning, and the statistical structure of language and images that
makes generation possible. Chapter~4 describes the transformer architecture
in full technical detail. By the end of those chapters, every equation
in this historical overview will have been derived from first principles.

\chapter{Mathematical Foundations for Generative AI}

\section{Introduction}

Generative AI systems, despite their seemingly magical capabilities, are built upon a foundation of relatively straightforward mathematical concepts. At their core, these models manipulate numbers—vast arrays of numbers—through operations that can be understood with high school mathematics and a bit of linear algebra. This chapter provides the essential mathematical toolkit needed to understand how generative AI systems work.

The mathematics we'll explore serves several critical purposes in generative AI. First, it provides the language for representing information: words become vectors, images become tensors, and probabilities govern generation. Second, it defines the operations models perform: transforming inputs through matrix multiplications, computing similarities via dot products, and selecting outputs using probability distributions. Third, it enables learning: gradient calculations tell us how to adjust billions of parameters to improve model performance.

We present this material as ``minimal math''—the essential concepts without unnecessary complexity. Our goal is practical understanding rather than mathematical rigor. If you can follow the examples and grasp the intuitions, you have sufficient mathematical background to understand modern generative AI.

\section{Scalars: The Building Blocks}

\subsection{Definition and Properties}

A scalar is simply a single real number—a quantity with magnitude but no direction. Scalars are the most basic mathematical objects we'll encounter, yet they pervade generative AI systems as individual weights, activation values, and probabilities.

Examples of scalars include:
\begin{equation}
3.23, \quad -2.47, \quad 0.435, \quad 10.54
\end{equation}

In generative AI contexts, scalars represent individual parameters (such as a single weight in a neural network), single probabilities (such as the likelihood of generating a particular token), or scalar functions (such as a loss value that measures model performance on a task).

Scalars follow the familiar arithmetic operations: addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division. When we scale a vector or matrix, we multiply each element by a scalar. When we compute a loss function, we typically obtain a scalar value that summarizes model performance. The simplicity of scalars belies their importance—billions of scalar parameters collectively encode the knowledge within large language models.

\section{Vectors: Ordered Collections of Numbers}

\subsection{Vector Representation}

A vector is an ordered array of real numbers, where the length of the array defines the vector's dimension. Vectors are fundamental to generative AI because they provide a way to represent complex objects—words, sentences, images, or any data—as points in high-dimensional space.

Consider a vector $\mathbf{x}$ of dimension $d = 4$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{x} = \begin{bmatrix}
3.23 \\
-2.47 \\
0.435 \\
10.54
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

This is a \textbf{column vector}, where elements are arranged vertically. The same vector can be written as a \textbf{row vector}:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{x}^T = \begin{bmatrix} 3.23 & -2.47 & 0.435 & 10.54 \end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

The superscript $T$ denotes the transpose operation, which converts columns to rows and vice versa.

\subsection{Vectors in Generative AI}

In language models, vectors represent word embeddings—numerical representations that capture semantic meaning. The word ``king'' might be represented as a 768-dimensional vector, where each dimension captures some aspect of meaning. Similar words have similar vectors, enabling models to understand relationships and analogies.

In image generation models, vectors can represent latent codes—compressed representations of images. A 512-dimensional vector might encode all the information needed to generate a detailed portrait, with different dimensions controlling features like age, gender, expression, and lighting.

The dimension of a vector determines its expressiveness. Higher dimensions allow more nuanced representations but require more computation. Modern language models use embedding dimensions ranging from hundreds to thousands, balancing expressiveness with efficiency.

\subsection{Vector Norm: Measuring Magnitude} \label{subsec:vector_norm}

The norm (or magnitude) of a vector quantifies its length—how far the vector reaches from the origin in its space. For a $d$-dimensional vector $\mathbf{x}$, the Euclidean norm (also called the L2 norm) is defined as:

\begin{equation}
\|\mathbf{x}\| = \sqrt{\sum_{i=1}^{d} x_i^2}
\end{equation}

This is simply the generalization of the Pythagorean theorem to arbitrary dimensions. For our example vector:

\begin{equation}
\|\mathbf{x}\| = \sqrt{3.23^2 + (-2.47)^2 + 0.435^2 + 10.54^2}
\end{equation}

Computing this yields approximately $\|\mathbf{x}\| \approx 11.16$.

\subsection{Unit Vectors and Normalization}

A \textbf{unit vector} is a vector with norm equal to 1. Unit vectors are important in generative AI for several reasons. They provide a standard scale for comparisons, they enable pure directional information without magnitude interference, and they stabilize certain mathematical operations.

To create a unit vector from any vector $\mathbf{x}$, we normalize by dividing each element by the vector's norm:

\begin{equation}
\hat{\mathbf{x}} = \frac{\mathbf{x}}{\|\mathbf{x}\|} = \frac{1}{\|\mathbf{x}\|} \begin{bmatrix}
x_1 \\
x_2 \\
\vdots \\
x_d
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

The normalized vector $\hat{\mathbf{x}}$ points in the same direction as $\mathbf{x}$ but has unit length. In language models, normalizing embeddings before computing similarities ensures that comparison depends only on direction (semantic relationship) and not magnitude (which might reflect word frequency or other artifacts).

\subsection{Linear Transformations}

A transformation $T$ is called a \textbf{linear transformation} if it satisfies two properties for all scalars $a$ and all vectors $\mathbf{x}$ and $\mathbf{y}$:

\begin{equation}
T(\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{y}) = T(\mathbf{x}) + T(\mathbf{y})
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
T(a\mathbf{x}) = aT(\mathbf{x})
\end{equation}

These properties capture the essence of ``linearity''—the transformation distributes over addition and commutes with scalar multiplication. Linear transformations are fundamental to neural networks because they're computationally efficient and mathematically well-behaved.

A simple example: scaling each element of a vector by 2 is a linear transformation. If $T(\mathbf{x}) = 2\mathbf{x}$, then $T(\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{y}) = 2(\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{y}) = 2\mathbf{x} + 2\mathbf{y} = T(\mathbf{x}) + T(\mathbf{y})$, and $T(a\mathbf{x}) = 2(a\mathbf{x}) = a(2\mathbf{x}) = aT(\mathbf{x})$.

Neural networks consist of layers of linear transformations (matrix multiplications) followed by nonlinear activation functions. The linear transformations provide computational efficiency and enable effective learning via backpropagation, while the nonlinear activations provide the expressive power needed to model complex functions.

\section{Probability Vectors and the SoftMax Function}

\subsection{Probability Vectors}

A \textbf{probability vector} is a special vector whose elements are real numbers between 0 and 1 that sum to exactly 1. Probability vectors represent probability distributions over discrete outcomes.

Example probability vector $\mathbf{p}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{p} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.80 \\
0.07 \\
0.01 \\
0.12
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

This vector might represent the probability distribution over four possible next words in a language model: the model assigns 80\% probability to the first word, 7\% to the second, 1\% to the third, and 12\% to the fourth.

Probability vectors satisfy two constraints:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Each element $p_i$ satisfies $0 \leq p_i \leq 1$
\item The sum of all elements equals 1: $\sum_{i=1}^{K} p_i = 1$
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{The SoftMax Function}

The SoftMax function provides a way to convert any real-valued vector into a valid probability vector. This transformation is ubiquitous in generative AI, appearing in the output layers of language models, attention mechanisms, and classification systems.

Given a real-valued vector $\mathbf{x}$ with $K$ elements, the SoftMax function $\sigma(\mathbf{x})$ produces a probability vector of the same dimension. The $i^{th}$ element is defined as:

\begin{equation}
\sigma(\mathbf{x})_i = \frac{e^{x_i}}{\sum_{j=0}^{d} e^{x_j}}
\end{equation}

where $e \approx 2.71828$ is Euler's number (the base of natural logarithms).

The SoftMax function has several important properties:

\textbf{Normalization:} The denominator normalizes the outputs so they sum to 1, satisfying the probability constraint.

\textbf{Exponentiation:} The exponential function ensures all outputs are positive (necessary for probabilities) and amplifies differences between inputs. Larger input values lead to larger output probabilities, but the relationship is smooth and differentiable.

\textbf{Temperature:} The function can be modified with a temperature parameter $\tau$: $\sigma(\mathbf{x}/\tau)_i = \frac{e^{x_i/\tau}}{\sum_{j=0}^{d} e^{x_j/\tau}}$. Higher temperatures make the distribution more uniform (more random generation), while lower temperatures make it more peaked (more deterministic generation).

\subsubsection{SoftMax Example}

Consider the real-valued vector:
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{x}^T = [0.85841407 \quad 0.03210117 \quad -0.0451724 \quad 0.76411179]
\end{equation}

Applying SoftMax yields:
\begin{equation}
\sigma(\mathbf{x}^T) = [0.3632689 \quad 0.15898827 \quad 0.14716536 \quad 0.33057747]
\end{equation}

Notice that:
\begin{itemize}
\item All values are between 0 and 1
\item The values sum to 1 (within numerical precision)
\item The largest input (0.858...) corresponds to the largest output probability (0.363...)
\item Even the negative input (-0.045...) produces a valid probability (0.147...)
\end{itemize}

In a language model, if these represented logits (pre-SoftMax scores) for four possible next words, the model would select the first word with 36.3\% probability, the fourth word with 33.1\% probability, and so on.

\section{Measuring Probability Distributions}

\subsection{KL-Divergence}

The Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence measures how different one probability distribution is from another. Given two probability vectors $\mathbf{p}$ and $\mathbf{q}$ with $K$ elements, where all elements of $\mathbf{q}$ are positive ($q_i > 0$), the KL-divergence is:

\begin{equation}
D_{KL}(\mathbf{p} \| \mathbf{q}) = \sum_{i=1}^{K} \left( p_i \ln p_i - p_i \ln q_i \right)
\end{equation}

This can be rewritten as:
\begin{equation}
D_{KL}(\mathbf{p} \| \mathbf{q}) = \sum_{i=1}^{K} p_i \ln \frac{p_i}{q_i}
\end{equation}

KL-divergence has several important properties:

\textbf{Non-negativity:} $D_{KL}(\mathbf{p} \| \mathbf{q}) \geq 0$, with equality only when $\mathbf{p} = \mathbf{q}$

\textbf{Asymmetry:} Generally $D_{KL}(\mathbf{p} \| \mathbf{q}) \neq D_{KL}(\mathbf{q} \| \mathbf{p})$

\textbf{Not a distance metric:} Due to asymmetry and failure to satisfy the triangle inequality

In generative AI, KL-divergence is used to measure how well a model's predicted distribution matches the true data distribution.

\subsection{Cross-Entropy}
\label{sec:cross-entropy}

Cross-entropy is a special case of KL-divergence that arises frequently in machine learning. When the true distribution $\mathbf{p}$ is a one-hot vector (exactly one element is 1, all others are 0), the KL-divergence simplifies to the cross-entropy:

\begin{equation}
H(\mathbf{p}, \mathbf{q}) = -\sum_{i=1}^{K} p_i \ln q_i
\end{equation}

\subsubsection{Cross-Entropy Example}

Consider:
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{p} = \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix}, \quad
\mathbf{q} = \begin{bmatrix} 0.75 \\ 0.05 \\ 0.20 \end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

Here $\mathbf{p}$ represents the true label (the third class), and $\mathbf{q}$ represents the model's predicted probabilities. The cross-entropy is:

\begin{equation}
H(\mathbf{p}, \mathbf{q}) = -(0 \cdot \ln 0.75 + 0 \cdot \ln 0.05 + 1 \cdot \ln 0.20)
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
H(\mathbf{p}, \mathbf{q}) = -\ln 0.20 \approx 1.609
\end{equation}

The cross-entropy depends only on the probability the model assigns to the correct class. If the model assigns high probability to the correct answer, cross-entropy is low (good). If the model assigns low probability to the correct answer, cross-entropy is high (bad).

In language model training, cross-entropy loss measures how well the model predicts the next token. For each position in the training text, we have a one-hot true distribution (the actual next token) and the model's predicted distribution over all possible tokens. Minimizing cross-entropy trains the model to assign high probability to the correct next token.

\section{Dot Products: Measuring Similarity}

\subsection{Definition and Computation}

The dot product (also called inner product) of two vectors with the same number of elements is computed by multiplying corresponding elements and summing the results. The dot product of two vectors always produces a scalar.

For a row vector $\mathbf{a}^T$ and column vector $\mathbf{b}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{a}^T = [3.0 \quad 1.0 \quad 4.0 \quad -0.5]
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{b} = \begin{bmatrix}
2.0 \\
-6.0 \\
0.25 \\
2.0
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

The dot product is:
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{a}^T \mathbf{b} = 3.0 \cdot 2.0 + 1.0 \cdot (-6.0) + 4.0 \cdot 0.25 + (-0.5) \cdot 2.0
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{a}^T \mathbf{b} = 6.0 - 6.0 + 1.0 - 1.0 = 0
\end{equation}

\subsection{Geometric Interpretation}

The dot product has a profound geometric interpretation. For vectors $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{a}^T \mathbf{b} = \|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\| \cos \theta
\end{equation}

where $\theta$ is the angle between the vectors. This reveals that the dot product measures both magnitude and directional alignment:

\begin{itemize}
\item When vectors point in the same direction ($\theta = 0$), $\cos \theta = 1$, and the dot product is maximally positive
\item When vectors are perpendicular ($\theta = 90^\circ$), $\cos \theta = 0$, and the dot product is zero (the vectors are orthogonal)
\item When vectors point in opposite directions ($\theta = 180^\circ$), $\cos \theta = -1$, and the dot product is maximally negative
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Dot Products in Generative AI}

For normalized vectors (unit vectors), the dot product directly gives the cosine of the angle, providing a similarity measure. This cosine similarity is ubiquitous in generative AI:

\textbf{Semantic similarity:} In language models, the dot product of normalized word embeddings measures semantic similarity. ``King'' and ``queen'' have high cosine similarity, while ``king'' and ``pencil'' have low similarity.

\textbf{Attention mechanisms:} The attention mechanism in transformers computes dot products between query and key vectors to determine which parts of the input to focus on. Higher dot products indicate higher relevance.

\textbf{Retrieval:} When retrieving relevant documents for a query, systems compute dot products between query embeddings and document embeddings, ranking by similarity.

The efficiency of dot product computation—it's a simple sum of element-wise products—makes it practical even for high-dimensional vectors, enabling transformers to scale to thousands of dimensions.

\section{Matrices: Linear Transformations}

\subsection{Matrix Structure and Notation}

A matrix is a two-dimensional array of numbers arranged in rows and columns. The shape of a matrix is specified as (num\_rows, num\_columns). Consider matrix $\mathbf{A}$ with shape $(4, 3)$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{A} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.550 & 0.377 & 0.194 \\
0.285 & 0.038 & 0.617 \\
0.566 & 0.842 & 0.715 \\
0.105 & 0.966 & 0.985
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

Matrices are denoted by bold capital letters. Individual elements are referenced by subscripts: $A_{ij}$ refers to the element in row $i$, column $j$.

\subsection{Matrix Transpose}

The transpose of a matrix $\mathbf{A}$, denoted $\mathbf{A}^T$, is obtained by interchanging rows and columns. If $\mathbf{A}$ has shape $(m, n)$, then $\mathbf{A}^T$ has shape $(n, m)$. The element at position $(i, j)$ in $\mathbf{A}$ appears at position $(j, i)$ in $\mathbf{A}^T$.

For our example:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{A}^T = \begin{bmatrix}
0.550 & 0.285 & 0.566 & 0.105 \\
0.377 & 0.038 & 0.842 & 0.966 \\
0.194 & 0.617 & 0.715 & 0.985
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

The transpose operation is fundamental in neural networks, particularly in backpropagation where gradients flow backward through transposed weight matrices.

\subsection{Matrices and Vectors}

A matrix can be viewed in two complementary ways:

\textbf{As stacked row vectors:} Each row of the matrix is a row vector. Matrix $\mathbf{A}$ consists of 4 row vectors stacked vertically, each with 3 elements.

\textbf{As stacked column vectors:} Each column of the matrix is a column vector. Matrix $\mathbf{A}$ consists of 3 column vectors placed side by side, each with 4 elements.

This dual perspective is important: when we multiply a matrix by a vector, we can think either of taking dot products with the matrix's rows or of taking a weighted combination of the matrix's columns.

Note that the $i^{th}$ row vector $\mathbf{a}_i^T$ and the $i^{th}$ column vector $\mathbf{a}_i$ are different vectors—they live in different spaces and serve different roles.

\section{Matrix Operations}

\subsection{Matrix Addition and Subtraction}

Matrix addition and subtraction are element-wise operations: matrices of the same shape are added or subtracted by combining corresponding elements. If $\mathbf{A}$ and $\mathbf{B}$ both have shape $(m, n)$, then $\mathbf{C} = \mathbf{A} + \mathbf{B}$ also has shape $(m, n)$, with $C_{ij} = A_{ij} + B_{ij}$.

These operations are commutative: $\mathbf{A} + \mathbf{B} = \mathbf{B} + \mathbf{A}$.

\subsection{Matrix Multiplication}

Matrix multiplication is more complex and non-commutative. Given matrices $\mathbf{A}$ of shape $(m, k)$ and $\mathbf{B}$ of shape $(k, n)$, the product $\mathbf{C} = \mathbf{AB}$ has shape $(m, n)$.

\textbf{Dimension requirement:} The matrices can only be multiplied if the number of columns in $\mathbf{A}$ equals the number of rows in $\mathbf{B}$.

\textbf{Element computation:} Element $C_{ij}$ is the dot product of the $i^{th}$ row of $\mathbf{A}$ and the $j^{th}$ column of $\mathbf{B}$:

\begin{equation}
C_{ij} = \mathbf{a}_i^T \mathbf{b}_j = \sum_{p=1}^{k} A_{ip} B_{pj}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Matrix Multiplication Example}

Consider:
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{A} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.678 & 0.390 & 0.022 \\
0.671 & 0.395 & 0.514 \\
0.262 & 0.250 & 0.014 \\
0.654 & 0.317 & 0.529
\end{bmatrix}, \quad
\mathbf{B} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.089 & 0.193 & 0.151 & 0.573 \\
0.267 & 0.056 & 0.704 & 0.939 \\
0.460 & 0.272 & 0.104 & 0.953
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

The product $\mathbf{C} = \mathbf{AB}$ has shape $(4, 4)$. Let's compute $C_{11}$:

\begin{equation}
C_{11} = \mathbf{a}_1^T \mathbf{b}_1 = 0.678 \cdot 0.089 + 0.390 \cdot 0.267 + 0.022 \cdot 0.460 \approx 0.175
\end{equation}

The complete result is:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{C} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.175 & 0.158 & 0.379 & 0.776 \\
0.402 & 0.291 & 0.433 & 1.246 \\
0.097 & 0.068 & 0.217 & 0.398 \\
0.387 & 0.287 & 0.377 & 1.176
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

Matrix multiplication is the fundamental operation in neural networks. Each layer performs a matrix multiplication (followed by a nonlinearity), transforming the input representation into a new representation that captures higher-level features.

\subsection{Matrices as Linear Operators}

A matrix $\mathbf{A}$ of shape $(m, n)$ can be viewed as a linear operator that maps vectors from $\mathbb{R}^n$ to $\mathbb{R}^m$. Given a column vector $\mathbf{x}$ of shape $(n, 1)$, multiplication yields:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{A}\mathbf{x}
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{y}$ has shape $(m, 1)$.

\subsection{Example of Matrix as Operator}

\begin{equation}
\begin{bmatrix}
0.7607 \\
1.3522
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
0.1378 & 0.5252 & 0.3610 \\
0.6697 & 0.8013 & 0.5315
\end{bmatrix}
\begin{bmatrix}
0.4582 \\
0.6547 \\
0.9797
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

The matrix transforms a 3-dimensional input vector into a 2-dimensional output vector. This transformation is linear—it preserves the operations of vector addition and scalar multiplication.

In neural networks, weight matrices serve as learned linear transformations. A language model might have a weight matrix that transforms 768-dimensional word embeddings into 3072-dimensional hidden representations. The network learns the optimal transformation through training on text data.

\section{Calculus for Neural Networks}

\subsection{The Gradient Concept}

Neural networks learn by computing gradients—measures of how a function changes when its inputs change. Gradients tell us which direction to adjust parameters to improve performance.

\subsection{Gradient of Scalar Function with Respect to Scalar}

For a scalar function $f(x)$ with respect to a scalar variable $x$ at value $x_0$, the gradient is simply the derivative from calculus:

\begin{equation}
\left(\frac{df(x)}{dx}\right)_{x_0} = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f(x_0 + h) - f(x_0)}{h}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Practical Gradient Computation}

For practical computation, we don't need to remember calculus formulas. We can approximate the derivative by choosing a small value $h$ (such as 0.0001) and computing:

\begin{equation}
\frac{df(x)}{dx} \bigg|_{x_0} \approx \frac{f(x_0 + h) - f(x_0)}{h}
\end{equation}

\textbf{Example:} Let $f(x) = x^2$ and compute the gradient at $x_0 = 0.25$ with $h = 0.0001$:

\begin{equation}
f(x_0 + h) = (0.25 + 0.0001)^2 = 0.06255001
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
f(x_0) = (0.25)^2 = 0.0625
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\frac{df}{dx}\bigg|_{x_0} \approx \frac{0.06255001 - 0.0625}{0.0001} = 0.5001
\end{equation}

This approximates the true answer of $0.5$ excellently. This numerical differentiation approach, while inefficient for training large models, provides intuition for what gradients measure: the rate of change of the function.

\subsection{Chain Rule}
\label{sec:chain-rule}

The chain rule is fundamental to backpropagation in neural networks. If $y = g(x)$ is a scalar function of $x$, and $h(y)$ is a scalar function of $y$, then:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:chain-rule}
\frac{dh(y)}{dx}\bigg|_{x_0} = \frac{dh(y)}{dy}\bigg|_{y_0} \cdot \frac{dy}{dx}\bigg|_{x_0}
\end{equation}

where $y_0 = g(x_0)$.

\textbf{Example:} Let $y = x^2$ and $h(y) = \ln y$. To compute $\frac{dh}{dx}$ at $x_0 = 0.25$ with $h = 0.0001$:

First, compute $y_0 = (0.25)^2 = 0.0625$.

Compute $\frac{dh}{dy}$:
\begin{equation}
\frac{h(y_0 + h) - h(y_0)}{h} = \frac{\ln(0.0625 + 0.0001) - \ln(0.0625)}{0.0001} \approx 16
\end{equation}

Compute $\frac{dy}{dx}$:
\begin{equation}
\frac{g(x_0 + h) - g(x_0)}{h} = \frac{(0.2501)^2 - (0.25)^2}{0.0001} \approx 0.5001
\end{equation}

Apply chain rule:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dh}{dx}\bigg|_{x_0} \approx 16 \times 0.5001 = 8.0016
\end{equation}

The true answer is $8$, so our approximation is excellent.

In neural networks, the chain rule allows gradients to flow backward through layers. If layer 2's output depends on layer 1's output, which depends on layer 1's weights, we use the chain rule to compute how the final loss depends on those weights.

\subsection{Scalar Functions of Vectors}

A scalar function of a vector $\mathbf{z}$ maps a vector to a single scalar value. Example: the dot product $f(\mathbf{z}) = \mathbf{w}^T \cdot \mathbf{z}$ where $\mathbf{w}^T = [w_1 \; w_2 \; ... \; w_K]$ is a constant row vector.

This function is scalar-valued because the dot product of two vectors produces a scalar.

\subsection{Gradient with Respect to Vectors}

The gradient of a scalar function $f(\mathbf{z})$ with respect to vector $\mathbf{z}$ is a vector of the same shape as $\mathbf{z}$:

\begin{equation}
\nabla_{\mathbf{z}} f(\mathbf{z}) = \begin{bmatrix}
\frac{\partial f}{\partial z_1} \\
\frac{\partial f}{\partial z_2} \\
\vdots \\
\frac{\partial f}{\partial z_k}
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

Each element $\frac{\partial f}{\partial z_i}$ is computed as the derivative of $f$ with respect to $z_i$ (treating other elements as constants), which we already know how to compute.

The gradient vector points in the direction of steepest increase of the function. In optimization, we move in the opposite direction (negative gradient) to minimize the function.

\subsection{Scalar Functions of Matrices}

A scalar function of a matrix $\mathbf{A}$ maps a matrix to a single scalar. In neural networks, the loss function is often a scalar function of the weight matrices.

Example: Let $\mathbf{A}$ be a variable matrix of shape $(m, n)$, $\mathbf{c}$ be a constant column vector of shape $(n, 1)$, and $\mathbf{p}$ be a constant probability vector of shape $(m, 1)$ with $p_i \in \{0, 1\}$. Define:

\begin{equation}
f(\mathbf{A}) = H(\mathbf{p}, \sigma(\mathbf{Ac}))
\end{equation}

This composes cross-entropy $H$ with SoftMax $\sigma$ applied to the matrix-vector product $\mathbf{Ac}$. The result is a scalar that measures how well the matrix $\mathbf{A}$ transforms vector $\mathbf{c}$ to match the target distribution $\mathbf{p}$.

\subsection{Gradient with Respect to Matrices}

The gradient of scalar function $f(\mathbf{A})$ with respect to matrix $\mathbf{A}$ is a matrix of the same shape as $\mathbf{A}$. For a $(3, 3)$ matrix:

\begin{equation}
\nabla_{\mathbf{A}} f(\mathbf{A}) = \begin{bmatrix}
\frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{11}} & \frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{12}} & \frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{13}} \\
\frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{21}} & \frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{22}} & \frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{23}} \\
\frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{31}} & \frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{32}} & \frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{33}}
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}

Each element $\frac{\partial f}{\partial A_{ij}}$ indicates how the function changes when that specific weight changes, computed using the same numerical approximation method.

In training neural networks, we compute gradients of the loss with respect to all weight matrices, then update each weight by moving slightly in the negative gradient direction. This gradient descent process iteratively improves the model's performance.

\section{Tensors: Generalizing to Higher Dimensions}

\subsection{Tensor Definition}

Tensors generalize scalars, vectors, and matrices to arbitrary dimensions. A tensor is simply an array of numbers with zero or more dimensions:

\begin{itemize}
\item 0-dimensional tensor: scalar
\item 1-dimensional tensor: vector
\item 2-dimensional tensor: matrix
\item 3-dimensional tensor: could represent an RGB image (height × width × color channels)
\item 4-dimensional tensor: could represent a batch of images (batch\_size × height × width × channels)
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Tensor Shape and Visualization}

Consider a 3-dimensional tensor with shape $(3, 2, 5)$. This can be visualized in multiple ways:

\textbf{As stacked matrices:} Three matrices of shape $(2, 5)$ stacked along the first dimension.

\textbf{As a grid:} A $3 \times 2$ grid where each cell contains a vector of length 5.

Both visualizations are valid—tensors can be conceptualized in whatever way best suits the problem. The tensor contains $3 \times 2 \times 5 = 30$ total elements, arranged in a 3-dimensional structure.

\subsection{Tensors in Generative AI}

Tensors are ubiquitous in modern AI:

\textbf{Transformer models:} Process sequences as 3D tensors (batch\_size, sequence\_length, embedding\_dimension)

\textbf{Image models:} Process images as 4D tensors (batch\_size, height, width, channels)

\textbf{Attention patterns:} Stored as 4D tensors (batch\_size, num\_heads, sequence\_length, sequence\_length)

Modern deep learning frameworks (PyTorch, TensorFlow) provide efficient tensor operations that run on GPUs, enabling parallel processing of these high-dimensional structures. Understanding tensor shapes and operations is essential for implementing and debugging generative AI systems.

\section{Conclusion}

This chapter has covered the minimal mathematical foundations needed to understand generative AI systems. We've progressed from simple scalars to high-dimensional tensors, from basic dot products to matrix operations, and from simple derivatives to gradients of complex functions.

The key insights to retain are:

\textbf{Representation:} Vectors and tensors represent data—words become embeddings, images become pixel arrays, and probability distributions become vectors.

\textbf{Transformation:} Matrices implement learned transformations that map inputs to outputs through layers of a neural network.

\textbf{Similarity:} Dot products measure similarity and attention, enabling models to find relevant information and make analogies.

\textbf{Optimization:} Gradients point the way to improvement, allowing models to learn from billions of examples through backpropagation.

\textbf{Probability:} SoftMax and cross-entropy connect continuous computations to discrete decisions, enabling generation of text, images, and other structured outputs.

These mathematical tools, while relatively simple individually, combine to enable the remarkable capabilities of modern generative AI. As we proceed through subsequent chapters, we'll see how these foundations support specific architectures like transformers, training procedures like supervised learning and reinforcement learning, and applications from text generation to image synthesis.

The mathematics of generative AI is fundamentally accessible—it builds on familiar concepts and can be understood through concrete examples and numerical computation. With this foundation in place, we're ready to explore how these mathematical operations combine to create systems that can write, draw, code, and reason.

\chapter{Core Concepts of Generative AI}

\section{Introduction}

Generative AI represents a fundamental shift in how we think about artificial
intelligence. Rather than simply analyzing or classifying existing data,
generative models create new content --- text, images, video, molecular
structures, and more. Understanding what it means to ``generate'' requires
grasping several interconnected concepts: the mathematical representation of
outputs, the probabilistic nature of generation, the role of conditioning
information, and the relationship between training data and learned
distributions.

This chapter establishes the conceptual foundations that underlie all generative
AI systems. We explore what these systems generate, what generation means
mathematically, how conditioning enables controlled generation, and how training
datasets allow models to approximate unknown data distributions. These concepts
apply universally across modalities --- whether generating text, photorealistic
images, or novel protein structures.

A key theme is that the mathematics of generation has deep historical roots.
Long before neural networks, researchers discovered that sequences of symbols ---
letters, words, musical notes --- obey statistical regularities that can be
captured with probability theory. Following that lineage from its origins to
modern large-scale models reveals not just how generative AI works, but why it
works the way it does.

By framing generation as probabilistic sampling from learned distributions, we
provide a unified perspective that encompasses diverse generative AI
architectures. This probabilistic view explains both the creativity of generative
models (they can produce novel outputs by sampling from a distribution) and their
occasional failures (sampling from a learned approximation introduces
uncertainty). Understanding these foundations is essential for both using
generative AI effectively and reasoning clearly about its capabilities and limits.

\section{What Does Generative AI Generate?}

\subsection{The Mathematical Representation of Outputs}

At its core, generative AI produces structured data represented as vectors or
tensors of numbers. While the semantic content varies dramatically across
modalities --- a poem, a portrait, a protein --- the mathematical representation
follows a common pattern: a $d$-dimensional vector $\mathbf{z}$ of values.

For most modalities, this output vector contains real numbers:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{R}^d
\end{equation}

The dimension $d$ depends on the modality and the specific task, ranging from
hundreds for short text sequences to millions for high-resolution images. We use
$\mathbf{z}$ throughout this chapter as a general placeholder for any generated
output regardless of modality. Later chapters introduce modality-specific
notation where greater precision is needed --- for example, when discussing
language models, individual tokens drawn from a vocabulary are written as
$v_i \in \mathcal{V}$ rather than the generic $z_i$.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{images/output_modalities.png}
    \caption{Generative AI outputs across modalities. In every case the output
    is a structured vector or tensor of numbers: integer token IDs for text,
    real-valued pixel intensities for images, $(x, y, z)$ atomic coordinates
    for molecules. The dimension $d$ varies from thousands (text) to millions
    (images) to tens of millions (video).}
    \label{fig:output_modalities}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Text Generation}

For text generation, the output has a special structure. Text consists of
discrete tokens --- words, subwords, or characters --- drawn from a finite
vocabulary. A language model generates a sequence of token identifiers, making
the output a vector of natural numbers:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{N}^d
\end{equation}

Here $d$ represents the number of generated tokens. Each element $z_i$ is an
integer indexing into the model's vocabulary (typically 32,000 to 100,000 tokens
for modern language models).

\textbf{Example:} Consider generating the sentence ``The cat sat on the mat.''
A tokenizer might represent this as token IDs $[450, 2472, 3332, 319, 262,
2603]$, making $d = 6$. The model generates this sequence one token at a time,
predicting each token based on all previous tokens.

The dimension $d$ is variable --- it represents the length of the generated
text, which can range from a single token to thousands of tokens for long-form
content. Modern language models use special tokens to mark sequence boundaries:
a beginning-of-sequence token and an end-of-sequence token that signals
generation completion.

\subsection{Image Generation}

For image generation, the output is a three-dimensional tensor representing pixel
values across spatial dimensions and color channels. An image of height $H$
pixels and width $W$ pixels with three color channels (red, green, blue) has:

\begin{equation}
d = H \times W \times 3
\end{equation}

\textbf{Example:} A $512 \times 512$ RGB image has $d = 512 \times 512 \times
3 = 786{,}432$ values. Each value typically represents a pixel intensity,
normalized to $[0, 1]$ or $[-1, 1]$.

Modern image generation models often work in a compressed \emph{latent space}
of much lower dimension --- for instance $64 \times 64 \times 4 = 16{,}384$
values for a $512 \times 512$ image --- and use a learned decoder to expand the
latent representation back to full resolution. This compression makes generation
computationally tractable while preserving semantic content.

\subsection{Video Generation}

Video extends images with a temporal dimension. A video with $T$ frames, each of
height $H$ and width $W$ with RGB channels, has:

\begin{equation}
d = T \times H \times W \times 3
\end{equation}

\textbf{Example:} A 10-second clip at 24 frames per second with $256 \times 256$
resolution has $d = 240 \times 256 \times 256 \times 3 \approx 47$ million
values. The enormous dimensionality makes video generation significantly more
challenging than image generation, requiring temporal compression, latent-space
techniques, and careful modeling of motion consistency across frames.

\subsection{Molecular Structure Generation}

In computational biology, generative models create three-dimensional molecular
structures. For a molecule with $N$ atoms, each atom's position in 3D space is
represented by three coordinates $(x, y, z)$, giving:

\begin{equation}
d = N \times 3
\end{equation}

\textbf{Example:} A small protein with 100 amino acids might have roughly 1,000
heavy atoms, yielding $d = 3{,}000$ coordinates. Beyond atomic positions,
molecular generation may include atom types (carbon, nitrogen, oxygen), bond
information, and conformational details. Crucially, generated structures must
satisfy physical constraints --- atoms cannot overlap, bond lengths and angles
must be chemically realistic, and the overall structure should be energetically
stable --- making validity a hard requirement rather than a soft aesthetic
preference.

\section{What Does It Mean to Generate?}

\subsection{Generation as Probabilistic Sampling}

Generation is fundamentally a probabilistic process. When we ask a generative
model to create content, we are requesting a sample from a probability
distribution over possible outputs. Mathematically, to generate means to sample
$\mathbf{z}$ from a data probability distribution $p_{data}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z} \sim p_{data}
\end{equation}

The notation ``$\sim$'' means ``is sampled from'' or ``is drawn from'' the
distribution. To understand why this probabilistic framing is the right one ---
and where it comes from --- it helps to trace the idea to its origins.

\subsection{The Statistical Origins of Generation: Markov and Shannon}

\subsubsection*{Markov and the Chain of Probability}

In 1913, the Russian mathematician Andrei Markov undertook a painstaking
analysis of Alexander Pushkin's novel-in-verse \textit{Eugene Onegin}. Working
entirely by hand, Markov laboriously counted the transitions between vowels and
consonants across roughly 20,000 successive letters of the text~\cite{markov1913}. His
goal was not literary criticism but a mathematical one: to demonstrate that the
letters of natural language are not independent of one another. He wanted to
show that the probability of the next letter depends on what came before it.

Markov's findings confirmed his hypothesis. In Russian text, vowels and
consonants do not alternate randomly; they cluster in patterns governed by the
language's phonological rules. The probability of a vowel appearing next is
substantially higher if the preceding letter was a consonant, and lower if it
was already a vowel. This dependency could be captured precisely by a
\emph{conditional probability}: the probability of the next symbol given the
current one.

What Markov had discovered --- and proved rigorously --- was that a sequence
of symbols could be modeled as a chain of conditional probabilities, where each
step depends on the previous state. We now call these \emph{Markov chains}.
The key equation is simple: if $z_i$ denotes the $i$-th symbol in a sequence,
then the probability of the next symbol depends only on the current one:

\begin{equation}
p(z_{i+1} \mid z_1, z_2, \ldots, z_i) = p(z_{i+1} \mid z_i)
\end{equation}

This is the \emph{Markov property}: the future depends on the past only through
the present state. With it, the joint probability of an entire sequence
factorizes neatly into a product of conditional probabilities:

\begin{equation}
p(\mathbf{z}) = p(z_1) \prod_{i=1}^{T-1} p(z_{i+1} \mid z_i)
\end{equation}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/markov_chain.png}
    \caption{A Markov chain over vowels (V) and consonants (C), inspired by
    Markov's 1913 analysis of \textit{Eugene Onegin}. Arrows show transition
    probabilities: the probability of the next letter type depends only on the
    current letter type, not on the full preceding sequence. This simple
    conditional structure produces realistic-looking sequences of letters
    despite its simplicity.}
    \label{fig:markov_chain}
\end{figure}

Markov's insight was profound but limited. The Markov property assumes that
only the immediately preceding symbol matters --- a strong simplification that
fails to capture longer-range structure in language. In English, whether a
sentence ends with a period or a question mark depends on words from much
earlier in the sentence, not just the immediately preceding word.

\subsubsection*{Shannon and the Entropy of Language}

Thirty-five years after Markov, the American mathematician and electrical
engineer Claude Shannon published \textit{A Mathematical Theory of
Communication}~\cite{shannon1948}, one of the most consequential scientific
papers of the twentieth century. Shannon was working on the problem of
transmitting messages efficiently and reliably, and to do so he needed a
rigorous way to measure the information content of a message.

Shannon's central insight was that information is intimately connected to
surprise: a symbol that is unlikely carries more information than a symbol that
is expected. He formalized this intuition with the concept of \emph{entropy},
which measures the average uncertainty in a probability distribution. For a
random variable taking values in a vocabulary $\mathcal{V}$ with probabilities
$p(v)$, the entropy is:

\begin{equation}
H = -\sum_{v \in \mathcal{V}} p(v) \log_2 p(v)
\end{equation}

Entropy is measured in bits when the logarithm is base 2. A fair coin has
entropy of 1 bit (maximum uncertainty for a binary choice). English text has
far less than the maximum possible entropy because the language is highly
redundant --- knowing the preceding letters sharply constrains what comes next.

To estimate the entropy of English, Shannon ran a now-famous experiment. He
asked human subjects to guess the next letter in a passage of text, recording
how many guesses it took them to get it right. When subjects had seen the
preceding context, they could often guess correctly on the first try ---
demonstrating that the conditional probability of the next letter given the
context is very high for predictable letters, and lower for surprising ones.
Shannon estimated the entropy of English at roughly 0.6 to 1.3 bits per letter,
far below the 4.7 bits that would result if all 27 symbols (26 letters plus
space) were equally likely.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/shannon_prediction.png}
    \caption{Shannon's conditional probability view of language. Given the
    context ``The cat sat on the '', the probability distribution over
    the next word is highly concentrated: ``mat'' is very likely, ``floor''
    is plausible, and ``banana'' has near-zero probability. The entropy of
    this conditional distribution is low because the context is highly
    informative. Modern language models learn precisely this conditional
    distribution at scale.}
    \label{fig:shannon_prediction}
\end{figure}

What Shannon gave us, beyond entropy, was a rigorous mathematical framework
for conditional probability in language. The probability of any text sequence
factorizes as:

\begin{equation}
p(\mathbf{z}) = \prod_{i=1}^{T} p(z_i \mid z_1, z_2, \ldots, z_{i-1})
\end{equation}

This is the \emph{chain rule of probability} applied to sequences. Unlike
Markov's assumption, this factorization is exact --- it makes no approximation.
The challenge is that computing $p(z_i \mid z_1, \ldots, z_{i-1})$ requires
conditioning on an arbitrarily long history, which becomes intractable as
sequences grow long. Shannon acknowledged this difficulty and proposed
approximate methods, but the computational tools to solve it at scale did not
yet exist.

\subsubsection*{The Bridge to Modern Generative Models}

Markov showed that sequences could be modeled with conditional probabilities.
Shannon showed that language has a measurable statistical structure and that
conditional probability is the right mathematical language for capturing it.
Both were limited by the computational constraints of their era --- Markov by
hand computation, Shannon by the early state of digital computing.

Modern generative AI is, in a deep sense, the resolution of the problem both
men identified: learning $p(z_i \mid z_1, \ldots, z_{i-1})$ from data at
scale, without the Markov approximation, for sequences of arbitrary length.
Where Markov used a table of transition probabilities between two symbol
classes, a modern language model learns billions of parameters that encode
conditional distributions over tens of thousands of vocabulary items given
contexts of thousands of tokens. Where Shannon estimated entropy from human
guessing experiments, modern models minimize cross-entropy loss over trillions
of tokens of training data, directly optimizing the quantity Shannon defined.

The mathematical form of the objective has not changed since Shannon wrote it
down. What has changed is the capacity to approximate it --- from two-state
Markov chains, through n-gram language models, to the transformer-based
architectures described in Chapter~4.

\subsection{The Unknown Data Distribution}

The critical challenge of generative AI is that $p_{data}$ is unknown. We do
not have a closed-form expression for the distribution over all meaningful text,
all coherent images, or all valid protein structures. This distribution is
defined implicitly by what exists or what is meaningful:

\textbf{Text:} $p_{data}$ represents the distribution over all text that is
grammatical, factual, stylistically coherent, or otherwise meaningful by human
standards --- encompassing books, articles, code, conversations, and creative
writing in all their variety.

\textbf{Images:} $p_{data}$ represents all images that humans would recognize
as coherent --- photographs of the real world, artistic creations, scientific
visualizations, and everything in between.

\textbf{Molecular structures:} $p_{data}$ represents all molecular
configurations admissible by the laws of physics and chemistry --- structures
that are stable, energetically favorable, and chemically relevant.

The unknown nature of $p_{data}$ is what makes generative AI both challenging
and necessary. If we had an explicit formula for $p_{data}$, we could sample
from it directly. Instead, we must approximate it by learning from examples.

\subsection{Unconditional Generation}

When we sample from $p_{data}$ without any additional constraints, we perform
\textbf{unconditional generation}:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z} \sim p_{data}
\end{equation}

Unconditional generation produces outputs that are typical of the data
distribution but not directed toward any particular goal. Early generative
models often focused on unconditional generation --- demonstrating that a model
had learned the distribution --- before conditional generation became the
dominant practical paradigm.

\subsection{Quality and Diversity}

When sampling from $p_{data}$, two properties matter:

\textbf{Quality (fidelity):} Samples should be realistic and coherent ---
indistinguishable from examples drawn from the true $p_{data}$. Low-quality
samples exhibit artifacts, inconsistencies, or nonsensical content.

\textbf{Diversity (coverage):} Samples should span the full range of $p_{data}$,
not concentrate on a narrow subset. A model with poor diversity might generate
many near-identical outputs, effectively ignoring large regions of the
distribution.

These properties trade off in practice. A model that ``plays it safe'' by
generating only high-confidence outputs may achieve high quality at the expense
of diversity. A model that explores the full distribution may occasionally
produce low-quality samples. This tension connects directly to how generative
models are evaluated, which we return to in Section~\ref{sec:evaluation}.

\section{Conditional Generation}

\subsection{Definition and the Conditional Distribution}

Most practical applications of generative AI require \textbf{conditional
generation} --- producing content that satisfies a specified condition. Instead
of sampling from the unconditional distribution $p_{data}$, we sample from a
conditional distribution given some conditioning variable $\mathbf{y}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z} \sim p_{data}(\cdot \mid \mathbf{y})
\end{equation}

The conditioning variable $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{R}^k$ is typically a vector
encoding a user's prompt, a reference image, a constraint, or any other
information that should guide generation.

Before proceeding to examples, it is worth understanding what a conditional
probability distribution \emph{means}. The conditional distribution
$p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})$ answers the question: among all outputs
$\mathbf{z}$ that could plausibly be generated, which are consistent with the
condition $\mathbf{y}$? By Bayes' theorem:

\begin{equation}
p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})
    = \frac{p_{data}(\mathbf{z},\, \mathbf{y})}{p_{data}(\mathbf{y})}
\end{equation}

Outputs $\mathbf{z}$ that co-occur frequently with $\mathbf{y}$ in the data
distribution receive high conditional probability; outputs that rarely or never
co-occur with $\mathbf{y}$ receive low conditional probability. Given the prompt
``The capital of France is'', for instance, the token ``Paris'' co-occurs with
that context far more often than ``Tokyo'' or ``banana'' in any large text
corpus, so $p_{data}(\text{Paris} \mid \text{``The capital of France is''})$
is very high.

The unconditional distribution can always be recovered by averaging over all
possible conditioning values:

\begin{equation}
p_{data}(\mathbf{z})
    = \int p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})\, p_{data}(\mathbf{y})\, d\mathbf{y}
\end{equation}

In practice, conditioning variables and generated outputs are never explicitly
computed as probability values. Instead, neural networks learn to approximate
$p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})$ from training data, and generation is
implemented through iterative procedures that produce samples from the learned
approximation.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/conditional_generation.png}
    \caption{Unconditional versus conditional generation. Left: sampling from
    the full data distribution $p_{data}$ produces varied outputs with no
    particular direction. Right: conditioning on $\mathbf{y}$ (a text prompt,
    an image, or any other signal) concentrates the distribution on outputs
    consistent with that condition, allowing users to guide generation toward
    desired content.}
    \label{fig:conditional_generation}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Conditioning Across Modalities}

The mathematical form $p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})$ is the same
regardless of modality. What varies is the nature of $\mathbf{z}$ (the output)
and $\mathbf{y}$ (the condition). Table~\ref{tab:conditioning} summarizes the
principal conditioning patterns across modalities.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Conditioning patterns across generative AI modalities. In every case
the conditioning variable $\mathbf{y}$ is encoded into a vector and used to
restrict the output distribution to content consistent with the condition.}
\label{tab:conditioning}
\begin{tabular}{lll}
\hline
\textbf{Output modality} & \textbf{Conditioning variable $\mathbf{y}$} &
\textbf{Example} \\
\hline
Text ($\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{N}^d$)
    & Text prompt
    & ``Explain quantum entanglement'' \\
    & Image + text
    & Chart image + ``Summarize this figure'' \\
\hline
Image ($\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{H \times W \times 3}$)
    & Text description
    & ``A mountain lake at dusk, oil painting'' \\
    & Reference image
    & Input photo + ``Make this a watercolor'' \\
    & Spatial mask
    & Segmentation map specifying layout \\
\hline
Video ($\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{T \times H \times W \times 3}$)
    & Text prompt
    & ``A cat walking through a garden'' \\
    & Initial frame(s)
    & First frame + ``Animate this image'' \\
\hline
Molecule ($\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times 3}$)
    & Amino acid sequence
    & Protein sequence → 3D structure \\
    & Functional constraints
    & Target binding affinity, stability \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

In all cases, the conditioning variable is encoded into a numerical
representation --- a sequence of token embeddings for text prompts, a vector of
image features for reference images, a one-hot or learned embedding for discrete
constraints --- and this representation is provided to the model alongside the
generation process.

\section{Training Datasets and Learning Data Distributions}

\subsection{The Role of Training Data}

Since $p_{data}$ is unknown, we approximate it by learning from examples. The
training dataset consists of $N$ samples drawn from the true data distribution:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z}_1, \mathbf{z}_2, \ldots, \mathbf{z}_N \sim p_{data}
\end{equation}

These samples are assumed to be independent and identically distributed
