(i.i.d.) --- each drawn independently from the same $p_{data}$. The central
assumption of statistical learning applies: with enough representative samples,
we can learn a good approximation to $p_{data}$ that generalizes to new samples
not in the training set.

For conditional models, training data consists of pairs $(\mathbf{z}_i,
\mathbf{y}_i)$ --- outputs paired with their conditions. For example, a
text-to-image model trains on (image, caption) pairs; a protein structure model
trains on (structure, sequence) pairs. The quality and coverage of these pairs
determines how well the model learns $p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})$.

\subsection{Training Data Sources by Modality}

Training data sources vary by modality and scale:

\textbf{Text:} Language model training datasets include web-scraped text,
digitized books, scientific papers, code repositories, and conversational data.
Modern large language models train on trillions of tokens. Scale has grown
rapidly: models from 2020 trained on hundreds of billions of tokens; those from
2024--2025 train on ten trillion or more.

\textbf{Images:} Image generation models train on billions of image-text pairs
scraped from the internet. Data quality varies enormously, from professional
photography to amateur snapshots. Careful curation --- filtering duplicates,
removing low-quality content, balancing topics --- is as important as scale.

\textbf{Video:} Video datasets are substantially smaller than image datasets due
to storage and processing costs, and temporal consistency requirements make
curation more demanding. Models train on clips from diverse sources including
web video, films, and domain-specific recordings.

\textbf{Molecular structures:} The Protein Data Bank (PDB) contains over
200,000 experimentally determined protein structures. While small by text or
image standards, each structure provides rich three-dimensional information.
Models also train on computationally predicted structures and molecular
dynamics simulations.

\subsection{Dataset Size and Quality}

Scale and quality both matter. Larger datasets generally improve performance,
but a curated dataset of high-quality examples may outperform a larger dataset
contaminated with spam, misinformation, or near-duplicates. Key curation
strategies include:

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Quality filtering:} Removing spam, machine-generated text,
      and low-coherence content
\item \textbf{Deduplication:} Removing near-identical examples that would
      cause the model to memorize rather than generalize
\item \textbf{Domain balancing:} Ensuring broad representation across topics,
      languages, styles, and domains
\item \textbf{Safety filtering:} Removing harmful, abusive, or
      privacy-violating content
\end{itemize}

\subsection{From Data to Learned Distribution}

Training a generative model transforms a finite collection of examples into a
continuous probability distribution $p_{model}$ that approximates $p_{data}$.
The process involves five steps:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Collect and curate training data:} Assemble examples from
      $p_{data}$, apply quality filters, and construct conditioning pairs
      where needed.

\item \textbf{Define a model architecture:} Choose a neural network structure
      capable of representing the learned distribution (discussed in
      Chapter~4).

\item \textbf{Define a training objective:} Specify a loss function that
      measures how well $p_{model}$ approximates $p_{data}$. Common choices
      include cross-entropy loss for autoregressive models (minimizing the
      average negative log-probability assigned to training examples) and
      denoising loss for flow-based models.

\item \textbf{Optimize model parameters:} Use gradient descent to adjust the
      model's parameters --- ranging from millions to hundreds of billions ---
      to minimize the training loss.

\item \textbf{Validate generalization:} Evaluate on held-out data to confirm
      the model approximates $p_{data}$ rather than memorizing training
      examples.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/training_pipeline.png}
    \caption{The training pipeline for a generative model. A finite training
    dataset $\{\mathbf{z}_i\}$ sampled from the unknown $p_{data}$ is used to
    optimize the model's parameters until the learned distribution $p_{model}$
    closely approximates $p_{data}$. The gap between $p_{model}$ and $p_{data}$
    --- the approximation error --- determines where the model fails: outputs
    in regions of $p_{data}$ not well covered by training data will be
    generated poorly or not at all.}
    \label{fig:training_pipeline}
\end{figure}

The resulting $p_{model}$ is never a perfect replica of $p_{data}$ --- there is
always approximation error arising from finite data, limited model capacity, and
imperfect optimization. Understanding where $p_{model}$ deviates from $p_{data}$
--- which regions of the distribution the model covers well and which it covers
poorly --- is essential for understanding when and why generative models
fail.

\section{The Generative AI Pipeline}

\subsection{From Training to Generation}

The complete pipeline for a generative AI system spans data collection to user
interaction:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Data collection and curation:} Assemble training data, filter
      for quality, remove harmful content, and pair outputs with conditions
      where applicable.

\item \textbf{Preprocessing:} Tokenize text, normalize and resize images,
      augment data to increase effective diversity.

\item \textbf{Pretraining:} Train the base model on the large-scale dataset
      to learn $p_{model} \approx p_{data}$.

\item \textbf{Fine-tuning (optional):} Further train on curated high-quality
      data or task-specific examples to specialize the model.

\item \textbf{Alignment (optional):} Use techniques such as reinforcement
      learning from human feedback (RLHF) to align model outputs with human
      preferences and safety requirements.

\item \textbf{Inference optimization:} Quantize, distill, or otherwise
      compress the model for efficient generation at deployment scale.

\item \textbf{Deployment:} Serve the model via APIs or local deployment,
      managing latency, throughput, and cost.

\item \textbf{User interaction:} Accept prompts $\mathbf{y}$, sample
      $\mathbf{z} \sim p_{model}(\cdot \mid \mathbf{y})$, and return the
      generated output.
\end{enumerate}

Each stage presents its own engineering and research challenges, and the field
continues to innovate in all of them.

\section{Model Families}

Modern generative AI systems fall into two broad architectural families,
distinguished by how they represent and sample from $p_{model}$.

\subsection{Transformer-Based Autoregressive Models}

Transformer-based models~\cite{vaswani2017attention} decompose the joint
distribution over a sequence using the chain rule of probability ---
the same factorization that Shannon wrote down in 1948:

\begin{equation}
p_{model}(\mathbf{z})
    = \prod_{i=1}^{T} p_{model}(z_i \mid z_1, \ldots, z_{i-1})
\end{equation}

Generation proceeds one element at a time, left to right: the model samples
$z_1$, then samples $z_2$ given $z_1$, then $z_3$ given $z_1, z_2$, and so
on. At each step, the transformer architecture computes the conditional
distribution $p_{model}(z_i \mid z_1, \ldots, z_{i-1})$ by attending to all
previous elements simultaneously --- overcoming the limitation of Markov's
one-step memory by conditioning on the full history.

This autoregressive approach applies naturally to any discrete sequence:
text tokens, image tokens, audio tokens, or any other modality whose outputs
can be represented as a sequence drawn from a finite vocabulary. Chapter~4
describes the transformer architecture that makes this computation tractable
at scale.

\subsection{Flow-Based Generative Models}

Flow-based models take a fundamentally different approach. Rather than
factorizing the distribution autoregressively, they learn a continuous
transformation $F_\theta$ that maps a simple source distribution (typically
Gaussian noise $\mathbf{\epsilon} \sim \mathcal{N}(0, \mathbf{I})$) to the
data distribution $p_{data}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z} = F_\theta(\mathbf{\epsilon}), \qquad \mathbf{\epsilon} \sim
\mathcal{N}(0, \mathbf{I})
\end{equation}

Generation is a single forward pass through $F_\theta$: sample noise, apply
the learned transformation, obtain a data sample. Training learns $F_\theta$
such that the distribution of $F_\theta(\mathbf{\epsilon})$ matches $p_{data}$.

In practice, $F_\theta$ is typically decomposed into a sequence of $T$
incremental steps, each making a small adjustment to the current sample:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{z}_T = \mathbf{z}_0 + \int_0^T v_\theta(\mathbf{z}_t, t)\, dt
\end{equation}

where $v_\theta$ is a learned velocity field and $\mathbf{z}_0 = \mathbf{\epsilon}$
is the initial noise. The model learns to follow a flow from noise to data.
\emph{Diffusion models} are a closely related family that parameterize the
same transformation through a denoising process rather than a continuous flow;
they belong to the same broad genre and share the noise-to-data generation
paradigm.

Flow-based models excel at generating continuous, high-dimensional outputs
such as images and video, where the autoregressive token-by-token approach
would be prohibitively slow or would require lossy discretization.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{images/model_families.png}
    \caption{The two principal generative model families. Left: transformer-based
    autoregressive models generate sequences one token at a time, each conditioned
    on all previous tokens, following the chain rule of probability from left to
    right. Right: flow-based models learn a continuous transformation from
    Gaussian noise to the data distribution, generating the full output in a
    fixed number of iterative refinement steps from noise toward a coherent
    image or other continuous output.}
    \label{fig:model_families}
\end{figure}

\section{Evaluation Challenges}
\label{sec:evaluation}

Evaluating generative models presents a fundamental challenge because
``quality'' is inherently subjective and multidimensional. Unlike discriminative
tasks where accuracy has a clear binary definition, generative evaluation must
balance statistical fidelity with human perception and functional utility.

\textbf{Text generation.} Evaluation has moved beyond simple overlap metrics
like BLEU or ROUGE, which correlate poorly with human judgment on open-ended
tasks. Modern evaluation focuses on distinct dimensions: fluency and coherence
(measured via perplexity or large-scale benchmarks such as
HELM~\cite{liang2022helm}), factual accuracy (with metrics designed specifically
to detect hallucinations~\cite{lin2021truthfulqa}), and alignment with user
intent (typically assessed through human preference studies or reward
models~\cite{ouyang2022training}).

\textbf{Image generation.} The standard metric for realism and diversity is the
Fr\'{e}chet Inception Distance (FID), which measures the distributional
distance between generated and real images in a deep feature
space~\cite{heusel2017gans}. FID captures the quality-diversity tradeoff
introduced in Section~3.3 --- a model that generates only a few high-quality
images scores poorly on FID because the generated distribution does not cover
$p_{data}$. Prompt adherence is evaluated separately using CLIPScore, which
measures similarity between the generated image and the text prompt in a
shared embedding space~\cite{hessel2021clipscore}.

\textbf{Molecular generation.} Evaluation is grounded in physical validity
rather than aesthetics: generated structures must respect valency rules and
realistic bond geometries. Beyond validity, models are assessed on their ability
to optimize for target properties such as drug-likeness or binding
affinity~\cite{gomez2018automatic}, with standardized benchmarks such as
GuacaMol providing systematic comparison~\cite{brown2019guacamol}.

In all modalities, no single metric perfectly captures quality. Robust
evaluation combines automatic metrics with human expert review, accepting that
the approximation gap between $p_{model}$ and $p_{data}$ manifests differently
in different dimensions --- a model may be fluent but hallucinate facts, or
generate realistic-looking images that fail to follow the prompt.

\section{Conclusion}

This chapter has established the conceptual foundations of generative AI. We
defined what these systems generate (high-dimensional vectors representing text,
images, video, or molecules), what generation means (probabilistic sampling from
a learned approximation to an unknown data distribution), how conditioning
enables controlled generation (sampling from the conditional distribution
$p_{data}(\mathbf{z} \mid \mathbf{y})$), and how training datasets allow
$p_{model}$ to approximate $p_{data}$.

Several key insights emerge:

\textbf{Generation has deep mathematical roots.} The probabilistic view of
sequences traces directly to Markov (1913) and Shannon (1948). Modern generative
AI is the computational realization of their program --- learning conditional
probability distributions from data, at a scale neither could have imagined.

\textbf{Conditioning is what makes generation useful.} Unconditional sampling
from $p_{data}$ is theoretically elegant but practically limited. Conditioning
on a prompt, a reference, or a constraint transforms a curiosity into a tool.

\textbf{Approximation is unavoidable.} Every generative model learns
$p_{model} \approx p_{data}$ from finite data. The gap between them --- which
regions of the distribution the model covers well and which it covers poorly ---
determines where the model succeeds and where it fails.

\textbf{Two architectural families dominate.} Transformer-based autoregressive
models apply the chain rule of probability to discrete sequences; flow-based
models learn continuous transformations from noise to data. Both are described
in detail in subsequent chapters.

As we proceed to Chapter~4, we will build on these foundations to explore the
transformer architecture that underpins most modern generative models ---
seeing how the abstract conditional distributions defined here are concretely
approximated by self-attention, feedforward networks, and large-scale training.


\chapter{Model Architectures for Generative AI}

\section{Introduction}

The architecture of a generative AI model determines how it processes information, what patterns it can learn, and ultimately what it can generate. While Chapter 3 established that generative models learn to sample from data distributions, this chapter explores the concrete neural network structures that enable this learning. The transformer architecture, introduced in the landmark 2017 paper ``Attention Is All You Need,'' has become the foundation for nearly all modern generative AI systems, from language models like GPT and Claude to multimodal systems that process both text and images.

Understanding model architectures requires grasping several interconnected concepts: how inputs are converted to numerical representations (embeddings), how information flows through layers of the network (self-attention and feedforward layers), how the architecture enforces certain computational patterns (causal masking for autoregressive generation), and how training objectives shape what the model learns. This chapter provides a detailed technical explanation of these components, focusing on the three main transformer variants—encoder-only, decoder-only, and encoder-decoder architectures—and explaining when and why each is used.

The material builds directly on the mathematical foundations from Chapter 2. We'll see how matrix multiplications implement learned transformations, how dot products measure similarity in attention mechanisms, how softmax converts scores to probabilities, and how cross-entropy loss guides learning. By understanding these architectural details, you'll gain insight into both the capabilities and limitations of current generative AI systems.

\section{Overview of Transformer-Based Language Model Architectures}

\subsection{The Three Transformer Variants}

Modern large language models are built on three architectural patterns, each suited to different tasks:

\textbf{Transformer Encoder (Masked Language Models):} These models process entire sequences bidirectionally, allowing each position to attend to all other positions. Representative models include BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers), RoBERTa, ALBERT, DeBERTa, and XLM-RoBERTa. Encoders excel at understanding tasks like classification, named entity recognition, and question answering where the full context is available upfront.

\textbf{Transformer Decoder (Causal Language Models):} These models generate sequences autoregressively, predicting one token at a time with access only to previous tokens. This category includes all major generative language models: the Claude family (Anthropic), ChatGPT and GPT-4 (OpenAI), Llama family (Meta), Mistral, and most other conversational AI systems. Decoders are optimized for generation tasks where output is produced sequentially.

\textbf{Transformer Encoder-Decoder (Sequence-to-Sequence Models):} These combine an encoder that processes input sequences bidirectionally with a decoder that generates output autoregressively. Models include BART, T5, Pegasus, and various sequence-to-sequence architectures. Encoder-decoders excel at transformation tasks like translation, summarization, and structured generation where clear input-output separation exists.

\subsection{Architectural Trade-offs}

The choice between these architectures involves fundamental trade-offs:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Bidirectional vs. Causal Context:} Encoders see the full sequence, enabling richer representations but preventing sequential generation. Decoders see only previous tokens, enabling generation but with more limited context at each step.
    \item \textbf{Task Suitability:} Encoders are natural for discriminative tasks (classification, extraction), decoders for generative tasks (completion, conversation), and encoder-decoders for structured transformation (translation, summarization).
    \item \textbf{Efficiency Considerations:} For generation, decoder-only models are often more efficient because they cache previous computations (via KV caching), while encoder-decoders must process the entire input for each generated token.
\end{itemize}

The trend in recent years has strongly favored decoder-only architectures for large-scale language models. This reflects the recognition that with sufficient scale and appropriate training, causal language models can perform both understanding and generation tasks effectively, while providing simpler architectures and more efficient inference.

% ============================================================
% REWRITE: Transformer Encoder Architecture through MLP
% Replaces the existing content from \section{Transformer Encoder Architecture}
% through the end of \section{Multi-Layer Perceptron (Feedforward Network)}
% ============================================================

\section{Transformer Encoder Architecture}

\subsection{The Transformer: A Brief Orientation}

Before diving into the encoder's components, it is worth pausing to establish
what a transformer is and why it matters. Prior approaches to sequence modeling
processed tokens one at a time, accumulating information in a hidden state that
was updated at each step. This sequential processing was a fundamental
bottleneck: because each step depended on the previous one, training could not
be parallelized across the sequence, and long-range dependencies were difficult
to learn because information had to pass through many intermediate steps before
influencing a distant position --- each additional step compounding the risk of
gradients shrinking to nothing during backpropagation
(see Equation~\ref{eq:chain-rule}).

The transformer, introduced by Vaswani et al.\ in the landmark 2017 paper
\textit{Attention Is All You Need}~\cite{vaswani2017attention}, replaced
sequential processing entirely with a mechanism called \emph{self-attention},
which allows every position in a sequence to directly attend to every other
position in a single parallel operation. This has two consequences that
together explain the transformer's dominance. First, training is fully
parallelizable across sequence positions, which makes efficient use of modern
hardware. Second, the path length between any two positions is constant
regardless of how far apart they are in the sequence, making long-range
dependencies no harder to learn than short-range ones.

Every transformer layer combines two operations in sequence: self-attention,
which aggregates information across positions, and a feedforward network (MLP),
which transforms each position's representation independently. This pairing ---
one operation that mixes information across positions, one that processes each
position in isolation --- is repeated $N$ times as the signal passes through
$N$ stacked layers. The encoder architecture described in this section
instantiates this pattern for bidirectional sequence understanding.

\subsection{High-Level Structure}
\label{sec:encoder}

The transformer encoder processes input sequences into contextualized
representations. As shown in Figure~\ref{fig:encoder_arch}, the architecture
consists of:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Input embedding layer:} Converts token IDs to dense vectors.
\item \textbf{Position embedding layer:} Adds positional information to the
      unordered set of embeddings.
\item \textbf{$N$ stacked encoder layers:} Each applies self-attention and
      feedforward transformations.
\item \textbf{Output embeddings:} Contextualized representations of each input
      token.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{images/transformer_encoder_arch.png}
    \caption{The Transformer Encoder Architecture. Input tokens are converted
    to embeddings, summed with position embeddings, and passed through $N$
    transformer layers.}
    \label{fig:encoder_arch}
\end{figure}

We denote the sequence length as $s$ and the hidden dimension as $h$. An
input sequence of $s$ tokens is converted to a matrix of shape $(s, h)$ that
flows through the network.

\subsection{Embeddings: From Tokens to Vectors}

\textbf{Token Embeddings:} Each token in the vocabulary (typically
30,000--100,000 tokens) is associated with a learned embedding vector of
dimension $h$ (commonly 768, 1024, or larger). The embedding layer is
essentially a lookup table --- a matrix $\mathbf{E}_{token}$ of shape
$(\text{vocab\_size},\, h)$. Given input token IDs, we retrieve the
corresponding rows:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{X}_{token} = \mathbf{E}_{token}[\text{token\_ids}]
\end{equation}

This produces a matrix of shape $(s, h)$ where each row is the embedding of
one token.

\textbf{Position Embeddings:} Transformers have no inherent notion of sequence
order --- the self-attention mechanism treats the input as an unordered set.
To inject positional information, we add position embeddings. Modern models
often use learned position embeddings --- a matrix $\mathbf{E}_{pos}$ of shape
$(\text{max\_sequence\_length},\, h)$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{X}_{pos} = \mathbf{E}_{pos}[\text{position\_ids}]
\end{equation}

\textbf{Combined Input:} The final input to the encoder is the sum of token
and position embeddings:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{X}_{input} = \mathbf{X}_{token} + \mathbf{X}_{pos}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Encoder Layer Structure}

Each encoder layer consists of two sub-layers, each wrapped with a residual
connection and layer normalization, as illustrated in
Figure~\ref{fig:encoder_block_detail}:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Multi-head self-attention
\item Layer normalization with residual connection
\item Multi-layer perceptron (MLP) / feedforward network
\item Layer normalization with residual connection
\end{enumerate}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.6\textwidth]{images/encoder_layer_detail.png}
    \caption{Detailed view of a single Transformer Encoder layer, featuring
    Multi-head Self Attention followed by a Multi-layer Perceptron (MLP),
    with LayerNorm and residual connections.}
    \label{fig:encoder_block_detail}
\end{figure}

Mathematically, for input $\mathbf{X}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{X}' = \text{LayerNorm}(\mathbf{X} + \text{MultiHeadAttention}(\mathbf{X}))
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{X}'' = \text{LayerNorm}(\mathbf{X}' + \text{MLP}(\mathbf{X}'))
\end{equation}

The \textbf{residual connections} (the ``$+$'' operations) add the sublayer's
input directly to its output before normalization. This allows gradients to
flow directly through the network during training, bypassing the sublayer
entirely if needed, and makes it possible to train very deep stacks of layers
that would otherwise suffer from vanishing gradients owing to chain rule (Section~\ref{sec:chain-rule}).

\subsubsection*{Layer Normalization}

\textbf{Layer normalization (LayerNorm)}~\cite{ba2016layernorm} stabilizes the
activations at each layer. As a $s \times h$ matrix  passes through $N$ stacked
transformations, the scale of its activations can drift --- growing very large
(causing gradient explosion) or collapsing toward zero (causing gradient
vanishing). The vanishing gradient problem is a direct consequence of the chain
rule (Equation~\ref{eq:chain-rule}): when gradients are multiplied through
many layers with each factor $ < 1 $, it shrinks the gradients, eventually making
it too small to update the early layers of the network. LayerNorm prevents this
drift by normalizing each token's hidden representation across the $h$ feature
dimensions to have zero mean and unit variance, then applying learned scale and
shift parameters $\boldsymbol{\gamma}$ and $\boldsymbol{\beta}$:

\begin{equation}
\text{LayerNorm}(\mathbf{x}) =
    \boldsymbol{\gamma} \odot
    \frac{\mathbf{x} - \mu}{\sigma + \epsilon}
    + \boldsymbol{\beta}
\end{equation}

where $\mu = \frac{1}{h}\sum_{j=1}^{h} x_j$ is the mean of the $h$ elements,
$\sigma = \sqrt{\frac{1}{h}\sum_{j=1}^{h}(x_j - \mu)^2}$ is their standard
deviation, $\epsilon$ is a small constant for numerical stability, and
$\odot$ denotes elementwise multiplication. Crucially, this normalization
operates on each token's representation independently --- the statistics
$\mu$ and $\sigma$ are computed over the $h$ features of a single token,
not across the batch or across sequence positions. This makes LayerNorm
invariant to batch size and sequence length, which are both variable in
language model training.

The learned parameters $\boldsymbol{\gamma}$ and $\boldsymbol{\beta}$, both
of shape $(h,)$, allow the model to restore any scale and shift that is
useful after normalization --- LayerNorm does not force the representation into
a fixed range, it simply removes uncontrolled drift. Note that in the equations
above, LayerNorm is applied \emph{after} the residual addition (post-norm).
Modern large language models typically apply it \emph{before} the sublayer
(pre-norm), which further improves training stability at scale and is the
convention used in the decoder architectures described later in this chapter.

% ============================================================
\section{Multi-Head Self-Attention: The Core Mechanism}
% ============================================================

\subsection{Attention Intuition}

Self-attention is the operation that distinguishes transformers from all
prior sequence models. Rather than passing information from one token to
another through a chain of sequential steps, self-attention allows every
position to directly compare itself to every other position and gather
information from whichever positions are most relevant --- in a single
parallel operation over the entire sequence.

The mechanism is built around three learned linear projections of the input,
named for their roles in an information retrieval analogy:

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Query (Q):} What am I looking for?
\item \textbf{Key (K):} What do I offer?
\item \textbf{Value (V):} What information do I contain?
\end{itemize}

Each token projects itself into all three roles simultaneously. The attention
weight between two tokens is computed as the dot product of one token's query
vector with the other's key vector --- a measure of how relevant the second
token is to the first. These weights are then used to form a weighted sum of
value vectors, producing an output that blends information from across the
sequence in proportion to relevance.

\subsection{Multi-Head Architecture}
\label{sec:multi-head-attention}

Rather than computing a single attention function over the full hidden
dimension, transformers use \emph{multiple attention heads} in parallel.
As shown in Figure~\ref{fig:multi_head_attention}, the hidden dimension $h$ is
split across $n_{heads}$ heads, each of per-head dimension $d_h$, such that
$n_{heads} \cdot d_h = h$. Each head computes attention independently,
allowing different heads to specialize in different types of relationships:
one head might track syntactic dependencies, another semantic similarity,
another coreference, and so on.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{images/multi_head_attention.png}
    \caption{Multi-head Self Attention mechanism. Inputs are projected into
    Queries (Q), Keys (K), and Values (V). Scaled Dot-Product Attention is
    applied in parallel across heads and the results are concatenated. Number of heads is denoted by $a = n_{heads}$, and each head dimension is denoted by $d = d_h$, such that $ad = h$.}
    \label{fig:multi_head_attention}
\end{figure}

For each head $i$, the input $\mathbf{X} \in \mathbb{R}^{s \times h}$ is
projected into queries, keys, and values of dimension $d_h$:

\begin{equation}
\text{head}_i =
    \text{Attention}(\mathbf{X}\mathbf{W}_i^Q,\;
                     \mathbf{X}\mathbf{W}_i^K,\;
                     \mathbf{X}\mathbf{W}_i^V)
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{W}_i^Q, \mathbf{W}_i^K, \mathbf{W}_i^V \in
\mathbb{R}^{h \times d_h}$ are learned projection matrices specific to head
$i$. The attention computation within each head is scaled dot-product
attention:

\begin{equation}
\text{Attention}(\mathbf{Q}, \mathbf{K}, \mathbf{V})
    = \text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^\top}{\sqrt{d_h}}\right)
      \mathbf{V}
\end{equation}

The dot products $\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^\top$ produce an $(s \times s)$ matrix
of raw attention scores --- one score for every pair of positions. Dividing by
$\sqrt{d_h}$ prevents the dot products from growing large in magnitude as
$d_h$ increases, which would push the softmax into regions of very small
gradients. Softmax then converts the scores into a probability distribution
over positions, and the weighted sum over $\mathbf{V}$ produces the attended
output for each position.

The outputs from all heads (each of shape $(s, d_h)$) are concatenated along
the feature dimension and projected back to $h$:

\begin{equation}
\text{MultiHead}(\mathbf{X})
    = \text{Concat}(\text{head}_1, \ldots, \text{head}_{n_{heads}})\,
      \mathbf{W}^O
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{W}^O \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times h}$ is a learned output
projection. The concatenation restores the full hidden dimension $h$ before
the result is passed to the residual connection and LayerNorm.

% ============================================================
\section{Multi-Layer Perceptron (Feedforward Network)}
% ============================================================

After self-attention aggregates information \emph{across} positions, each
encoder layer applies a position-wise feedforward network that transforms
each position's representation \emph{independently}. These two operations are
complementary: attention decides what information to gather from the sequence;
the MLP decides what to do with it at each position. As depicted in
Figure~\ref{fig:mlp_structure}, the MLP processes each of the $s$ positions
with the same learned weights but with no interaction between positions.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{images/mlp_structure.png}
    \caption{The Multi-layer Perceptron (MLP) block. It projects the input to
    a higher dimension ($4h$), applies a GeLU activation, and projects back to
    the original dimension ($h$).}
    \label{fig:mlp_structure}
\end{figure}

The MLP consists of two linear transformations with a nonlinear activation
in between:

\begin{equation}
\text{MLP}(\mathbf{Z}) = \text{GeLU}(\mathbf{Z}\mathbf{W}^1)\, \mathbf{W}^2
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{W}^1 \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times 4h}$ expands each position's
representation to an intermediate dimension of $4h$, and
$\mathbf{W}^2 \in \mathbb{R}^{4h \times h}$ projects it back to $h$.
The $4\times$ expansion provides the capacity for the MLP to learn complex
nonlinear transformations of each token's representation. The GeLU
(Gaussian Error Linear Unit) activation introduces the nonlinearity; without
it the two linear layers would collapse into a single linear transformation
and the expansion would provide no additional expressive power. The MLP
accounts for roughly two-thirds of a transformer layer's parameters, with
the attention projection matrices accounting for the remaining third.


\section{Pre-training Encoder Models: Masked Language Modeling}

\subsection{Training Objective}

Encoder models are typically pre-trained with masked language modeling (MLM), introduced by BERT. The training procedure:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Randomly select 15\% of input tokens for masking
\item Of the selected tokens:
   \begin{itemize}
   \item Replace 80\% with a special [MASK] token
   \item Replace 10\% with random tokens from the vocabulary
   \item Leave 10\% unchanged
   \end{itemize}
\item Train the model to predict the original tokens at masked positions
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:} 
\begin{itemize}
\item Original: ``The dog is chasing the cat on a busy street with traffic.''
\item Token IDs: [2, 14, 1952, 25, 13258, 14, 2008, 27, 21, 4394, 446, 29, 2227, 9, 3]
\item Corrupted IDs: [2, 14, 1952, 4, 13258, 14, 2008, 27, 21, 4394, 1312, 29, 2227, 9, 3]
\item Labels: [-100, -100, -100, 25, -100, -100, -100, 27, -100, -100, 446, -100, -100, -100, -100]
\end{itemize}

The label -100 indicates positions where no loss is computed. Only masked positions contribute to the loss.

\subsection{Encoder Loss Function}
\label{sec:encoder-loss-function}

For each masked position, the model outputs a probability distribution over the
vocabulary via a linear layer and softmax:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{p} = \text{softmax}(\mathbf{h} \mathbf{W}_{vocab})
    \in \mathbb{R}^{\text{vocab\_size}}
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{h}$ is the encoder's output embedding at that position and
$\mathbf{W}_{vocab}$ is a learned projection matrix of shape
$(h,\, \text{vocab\_size})$. Each entry $p_v$ is the model's predicted
probability that the masked token is vocabulary item $v$.

The target is represented as a one-hot vector
$\mathbf{y} \in \{0,1\}^{\text{vocab\_size}}$, where $y_v = 1$ for the single
correct token $v^*$ and $y_v = 0$ for all other vocabulary items. The
cross-entropy (Section~\ref{sec:cross-entropy}) loss over all masked positions is:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{MLM}
    = -\sum_{i \in \text{masked}} \sum_{v=1}^{V} y_{i,v} \log p_{i,v}
    = -\sum_{i \in \text{masked}} \log p_{i,v^*_i}
\end{equation}

The second equality follows directly from the one-hot structure: because
$y_{i,v} = 0$ for every $v \neq v^*_i$, all terms in the inner sum vanish
except the one corresponding to the true token, leaving only
$-\log p_{i,v^*_i}$---the negative log-probability the model assigned to the
correct answer at masked position $i$.

This objective forces the model to use bidirectional context to predict masked tokens, resulting in rich contextual representations.

\textbf{Why This Works:} MLM pre-training teaches the model to understand language deeply. To predict a masked word, the model must use syntactic structure (grammar), semantic relationships (meaning), and world knowledge (facts). The learned representations transfer well to downstream tasks.

\section{Vision Encoder Transformers}
\label{sec:vit}

Encoder Transformers can be adapted to process images by converting spatial structure into
sequences. Unlike text, which is already a discrete sequence of tokens, images are
continuous 2D grids of pixels. The Vision Transformer (ViT)
\cite{dosovitskiy2020image} bridges this gap through \textit{patchification}:
dividing the image into a regular grid of small patches, converting each patch
into a vector, and treating the resulting sequence as the transformer's input---in
direct analogy to how words are converted into token embeddings in a language model
(Figure~\ref{fig:vit_patchify}). How image token sequences are combined with text in multimodal models depends on the architecture. The simplest approach — used in most modern systems — concatenates projected image embeddings directly into the text token sequence.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{images/vit_patchify.png}
    \caption{Standard ViT patchification. An input image is divided into a regular
    grid of $P{\times}P$ patches. Each patch is flattened into a vector of length
    $P^2 C$, then linearly projected to the model's hidden dimension $h$, producing
    a sequence of $N = HW/P^2$ patch embeddings analogous to text token embeddings.
    A learnable \texttt{[CLS]} token is prepended for classification tasks.}
    \label{fig:vit_patchify}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Patchification and Projection}

Given an input image of shape $(H, W, C)$---height, width, and number of channels
(e.g., $C=3$ for RGB)---the image is divided into a grid of $N = HW / P^2$
non-overlapping square patches, each of spatial size $P \times P$ pixels. A
typical choice is $P = 16$, which yields $N = 196$ patches for a
$224{\times}224$ image.

\textbf{Flattening.} Each $P \times P \times C$ patch is rearranged
into a one-dimensional vector by concatenating all pixel values, producing a
vector of length $P^2 C$. For $P{=}16$ and $C{=}3$, each patch becomes a
768-dimensional vector. Collecting all $N$ patches gives a matrix
$\mathbf{P} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times P^2 C}$.

\textbf{Linear Projection.} Each flattened patch is projected to the
model's hidden dimension $h$ via a shared learned weight matrix
$\mathbf{W}_{proj} \in \mathbb{R}^{P^2 C \times h}$:

\begin{equation}
    \mathbf{X}_{patch} = \mathbf{P} \cdot \mathbf{W}_{proj},
    \qquad \mathbf{X}_{patch} \in \mathbb{R}^{N \times h}
\end{equation}

This projection plays the same role as the token embedding lookup table in a
language model, mapping raw inputs to the hidden dimension used throughout the
transformer.

\textbf{Position Embeddings and CLS Token.} Since the flattened sequence
loses all spatial ordering, learnable 1D position embeddings are added to each
patch embedding. A special learnable \texttt{[CLS]} token is prepended to the
sequence; its final representation is used as the image-level embedding for
classification tasks.

\textbf{Why patches and not pixels?} Attending over every pixel of a
$224{\times}224$ image would require processing a sequence of 50,176 tokens,
making the quadratic cost of self-attention completely intractable. With
$P{=}16$, the sequence shrinks to 196 tokens---a 256$\times$ reduction---while
each patch still captures a meaningful local region.

\subsection{Recent Variants in Patchification}

Standard ViT patchification is rigid: it requires resizing every image to a fixed
square resolution, uses a fixed patch size, and discards the original aspect ratio.
This introduces distortion, wastes compute on padding, and loses spatial structure
that may be semantically meaningful. A wave of recent work addresses these
limitations.

\subsubsection{Aspect-Ratio Preserving Patchification (Pix2Struct)}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/pix2struct_patchify.png}
    \caption{Pix2Struct aspect-ratio-preserving patchification
    \cite{lee2023pix2struct}. Rather than resizing to a fixed square, the image
    is scaled so that the maximum number of $P{\times}P$ patches fits within a
    token budget $T_{max}$. The resulting variable grid uses a learnable 2D
    positional encoding indexed by row and column to preserve spatial layout.}
    \label{fig:pix2struct}
\end{figure}

Pix2Struct \cite{lee2023pix2struct} preserves the original image aspect ratio
by scaling the image to fit within a maximum sequence length budget $T_{max}$,
then extracting as many fixed-size $P \times P$ patches as possible without any
padding or distortion (Figure~\ref{fig:pix2struct}). The variable spatial layout (e.g., $r$ rows and $c$
columns, $r \neq c$) is encoded via a learnable 2D positional encoding that
explicitly represents each patch's row and column index, rather than a flat 1D
index. This is particularly valuable for document and chart understanding, where
the spatial arrangement of text and graphical elements is semantically critical
and distortion from forced square resizing causes significant information loss.

\subsubsection{Flexible Patch Sizes (FlexiViT)}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/flexivit_patchify.png}
    \caption{FlexiViT variable patch sizes \cite{beyer2023flexivit}. A single
    model is trained by randomly sampling a patch size at each step from a
    predefined set. A shared base weight matrix $\mathbf{W}_{base}$ is
    dynamically resized via interpolation to match the sampled $P$. Smaller $P$
    yields longer sequences and higher accuracy; larger $P$ yields shorter
    sequences and faster inference.}
    \label{fig:flexivit}
\end{figure}

FlexiViT \cite{beyer2023flexivit} trains a single model to handle multiple patch
sizes by sampling a patch size $P$ uniformly at random from a predefined set
(e.g., $\{8, 10, 12, \ldots, 48\}$ for a $240{\times}240$ image) at each
training step (Figure~\ref{fig:flexivit}). Rather than maintaining a separate projection matrix for each $P$,
FlexiViT maintains one base weight matrix $\mathbf{W}_{base}$ of fixed size and
resizes it via interpolation to match the current $P$. This parameter sharing
ensures adequate training of all resolutions without the memory and design
overhead of multiple separate matrices. At inference time, $P$ can be chosen
freely: a smaller $P$ produces longer sequences and higher accuracy, while a
larger $P$ reduces sequence length and speeds up inference, giving a smooth
tunable accuracy--compute trade-off within a single checkpoint.

\subsubsection{Native Resolution Processing (NaViT)}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/navit_patchify.png}
    \caption{NaViT Patch n' Pack \cite{dehghani2023navit}. Images of different
    resolutions and aspect ratios are patchified at their native resolution and
    their patch sequences are packed into a single training sequence. Attention
    masks (shown as block-diagonal structure) prevent patches from different
    images attending to one another, preserving per-image independence.}
    \label{fig:navit}
\end{figure}

NaViT (Native Resolution ViT) \cite{dehghani2023navit} eliminates fixed-resolution
resizing entirely (Figure~\ref{fig:navit}). Each image is patchified at its original resolution and aspect
ratio, and the resulting variable-length patch sequences from multiple images are
\textit{packed} into a single training sequence---a technique called
\textit{Patch n' Pack}, inspired by sequence packing in NLP. Attention masks enforce
a block-diagonal structure that prevents patches from different images attending to
one another, preserving per-image independence. This removes distortion completely,
improves training efficiency (no wasted compute on padding short images to a fixed
length), and at inference time allows resolution to be adjusted freely to trade
cost against performance.

\section{Transformer Decoder Architecture}

\subsection{Structural Differences from Encoders}

Decoder architectures resemble encoders but with one crucial modification: masked (causal) self-attention. This enables autoregressive generation (Figure \ref{fig:decoder_arch}).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Placeholder: Use the Decoder Architecture diagram from PDF Page 12
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/decoder_arch.png}
    \caption{Transformer Decoder Architecture. It utilizes Masked Multi-Head Self Attention to ensure the model cannot see future tokens during generation.}
    \label{fig:decoder_arch}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Masked Multi-Head Self-Attention}

The key difference lies in the attention mechanism. Decoders use \textbf{causal masking} (Figure \ref{fig:masked_attention}) to ensure each position can only attend to previous positions.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Placeholder: Use the Masked Attention diagram from PDF Page 13
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{images/masked_attention.png}
    \caption{Masked Multi-head Self Attention. A causal mask (upper triangular matrix of $-\infty$) is applied to the dot products before Softmax, preventing information flow from future tokens.}
    \label{fig:masked_attention}
\end{figure}

This is implemented by modifying the attention computation:

\begin{equation}
\text{Attention}(\mathbf{Q}, \mathbf{K}, \mathbf{V}) = \text{softmax}\left(\frac{\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^T + \mathbf{M}}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right) \mathbf{V}
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{M}$ is a causal mask matrix:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{M}_{ij} = \begin{cases}
0 & \text{if } i \geq j \\
-\infty & \text{if } i < j
\end{cases}
\end{equation}

Adding $-\infty$ to future positions ensures that after softmax, their attention weights are zero. Position $i$ can attend to positions $0, 1, \ldots, i$ but not to positions $i+1, i+2, \ldots$.

\textbf{Why Causal Masking?} This enforces the autoregressive property: when predicting token $i$, the model can only use tokens $0$ through $i-1$. This matches the generation setting where we generate tokens sequentially without knowing future tokens.

\subsection{Autoregressive Generation}

During generation, the decoder produces one token at a time:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Start with an initial prompt (or a start-of-sequence token)
\item Feed the prompt through the decoder to get output embeddings
\item Apply a linear projection and softmax to get a probability distribution
      over the next token
\item Sample a token from this distribution (or take the highest-probability
      token)
\item Append the generated token to the input and repeat
\end{enumerate}

Mathematically, let $v_i \in \mathcal{V}$ denote the vocabulary token at
position $i$, where $\mathcal{V}$ is the token vocabulary. The model learns to
factorize the joint probability of a sequence of $T$ tokens as a product of
conditional probabilities:

\begin{equation}
p(\mathbf{v}) = \prod_{i=1}^{T} p(v_i \mid v_1, \ldots, v_{i-1})
\end{equation}

Each factor $p(v_i \mid v_1, \ldots, v_{i-1})$ is the probability the model
assigns to token $v_i$ being the correct next token, given every token that
has appeared before it. The decoder computes this distribution at position $i$
by attending only to positions $1$ through $i-1$ (enforced by the causal mask
described above), projecting the resulting hidden state through $\mathbf{W}_{vocab}$,
and applying softmax to produce a probability over all $|\mathcal{V}|$ vocabulary
items. Generation then proceeds left to right: once $v_i$ is sampled or
selected, it is appended to the context and the process repeats for position
$i+1$.


\section{Pre-training Decoder Models: Causal Language Modeling}

\subsection{Training Data Preparation}

Decoder pre-training uses causal (autoregressive) language modeling. The training procedure is simpler than MLM:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Take a text sequence and tokenize it
\item Chunk into sequences of the model's maximum length
\item The input is the sequence, the labels are the sequence shifted by one position
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Text: ``Generative AI refers to a new class of artificial-intelligence algorithms used to generate content.''
\item Input IDs: [8645, 876, 9552, 10229, 284, 257, 649, 1398, 286, 11666, 12, 32683, 16113, 973, 284, 7716, 2695, 13]
\item Label IDs: [876, 9552, 10229, 284, 257, 649, 1398, 286, 11666, 12, 32683, 16113, 973, 284, 7716, 2695, 13, 632]
\end{itemize}

At each position, the model predicts the next token. Position 0 predicts token 1, position 1 predicts token 2, and so on.

\subsection{Decoder Loss Function}

The loss function for causal language modeling has the same cross-entropy form
derived in Section~\ref{sec:encoder-loss-function}, with one structural
difference: rather than summing only over the subset of masked positions, the
loss is computed at every position in the sequence, because every token is a
prediction target. Using the same notation as before---$\mathbf{p}_i$ for the
predicted distribution at position $i$, $\mathbf{y}_i$ for the one-hot target,
and $v^*_i$ for the true token at position $i$---the loss averaged over all
$T$ positions is:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{CLM}
    = -\frac{1}{T} \sum_{i=1}^{T} \sum_{v=1}^{V} y_{i,v} \log p_{i,v}
    = -\frac{1}{T} \sum_{i=1}^{T} \log p_{i,v^*_i}
\end{equation}

The only differences from $\mathcal{L}_{MLM}$ are the $\frac{1}{T}$
averaging factor and the fact that the outer sum runs over all $T$ positions
rather than the masked subset. The collapse from the double sum to the single
term $\log p_{i,v^*_i}$ follows from the one-hot structure of $\mathbf{y}_i$
by exactly the same argument as before.


\textbf{Why This Works:} Causal language modeling teaches the model the statistical structure of language. By predicting the next token across billions of examples, the model learns grammar, facts, reasoning patterns, and even some level of common-sense understanding. The simplicity of the objective (just predict the next token) belies the complexity of the knowledge required to minimize the loss effectively.

\subsection{Scaling Properties}

Decoder models exhibit remarkable scaling properties. As model size (number of parameters), dataset size, and compute budget increase, performance improves predictably following scaling laws:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L} \approx \left(\frac{N}{N_0}\right)^{-\alpha}
\end{equation}

where $\mathcal{L}$ is test loss, $N$ is the number of parameters, and $\alpha \approx 0.076$ \cite{kaplan2020scaling}. This predictable scaling has driven the trend toward ever-larger models.

While Kaplan et al. (2020) \cite{kaplan2020scaling} established $\alpha \approx 0.076$, later work like Chinchilla (Hoffmann et al., 2022) \cite{hoffmann2022training} suggests different optimal ratios for parameter vs. data scaling.

\section{Transformer Encoder-Decoder Architecture}

\subsection{Combining Encoder and Decoder}

The encoder-decoder architecture (Figure \ref{fig:enc_dec_arch}) combines both components: an encoder processes the input sequence bidirectionally, and a decoder generates the output autoregressively using Cross-Attention.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Placeholder: Use the Encoder-Decoder diagram from PDF Page 16
    \includegraphics[width=1.0\textwidth]{images/enc_dec_arch.png}
    \caption{Transformer Encoder-Decoder Architecture. The Encoder (left) processes the input. The Decoder (right) generates output, attending to the Encoder's output via Cross-Attention layers.}
    \label{fig:enc_dec_arch}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Cross-Attention}

In cross-attention (Figure \ref{fig:cross_attention}), the queries come from the decoder, while keys and values come from the encoder.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Placeholder: Use the Cross Attention diagram from PDF Page 17
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{images/cross_attention.png}
    \caption{Cross Multi-head Self Attention. Queries ($Q$) originate from the Decoder's auto-regressive path, while Keys ($K$) and Values ($V$) are projected from the Encoder's output.}
    \label{fig:cross_attention}
\end{figure}

In cross-attention:
\begin{itemize}
\item Queries ($\mathbf{Q}$) come from the decoder's self-attention output
\item Keys ($\mathbf{K}$) and Values ($\mathbf{V}$) come from the encoder's final outputs
\end{itemize}

Mathematically, for decoder hidden state $\mathbf{X}_{dec}$ and encoder outputs $\mathbf{Z}_{enc}$:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{Q} = \mathbf{X}_{dec} \mathbf{W}^Q
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{K} = \mathbf{Z}_{enc} \mathbf{W}^K
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{V} = \mathbf{Z}_{enc} \mathbf{W}^V
\end{equation}

The attention computation then proceeds as usual:

\begin{equation}
\text{CrossAttention}(\mathbf{X}_{dec}, \mathbf{Z}_{enc}) = \text{softmax}\left(\frac{\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^T}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right) \mathbf{V}
\end{equation}

This allows each decoder position to attend to all encoder positions, gathering relevant information from the input to inform generation.

\textbf{Example - Translation:} When translating "The cat sat on the mat" to French, the encoder produces contextualized representations of each English word. During generation of "Le chat s'est assis sur le tapis," cross-attention allows the decoder to focus on relevant English words when generating each French word. Generating "chat" (cat) would attend strongly to "cat," while generating "assis" (sat) would attend to "sat."

\subsection{Training Encoder-Decoder Models}

Encoder-decoder models are typically trained on paired sequences. Common pre-training objectives include:

\textbf{Span Corruption (T5):} Randomly corrupt spans of input text, then train the model to reconstruct them. For example:
\begin{itemize}
\item Input: ``Thank you for inviting [MASK] to your party [MASK] week.''
\item Target: ``me last''
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Denoising (BART):} Apply various corruption strategies (token masking, deletion, infilling, permutation) and train to reconstruct the original.

\textbf{Sequence-to-Sequence Tasks:} Directly train on task-specific pairs (source-target for translation, document-summary for summarization).

\textbf{Sequence-to-Sequence Loss Function:}
The loss has the same cross-entropy form established in
Section~\ref{sec:encoder-loss-function}, now applied to the $T_{target}$ tokens
of the target sequence. At each target position $i$, the decoder produces a
predicted distribution $\mathbf{p}_i \in \mathbb{R}^V$ over the vocabulary,
conditioned on both the encoder output $\mathbf{Z}_{enc}$ (via cross-attention)
and all previously generated target tokens. The one-hot target $\mathbf{y}_i$
identifies the true target token $v^*_i$, and the loss is:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{seq2seq}
    = -\sum_{i=1}^{T_{target}} \sum_{v=1}^{V} y_{i,v} \log p_{i,v}
    = -\sum_{i=1}^{T_{target}} \log p_{i,v^*_i}
\end{equation}

The only structural difference from $\mathcal{L}_{CLM}$ is that the sum runs
over the $T_{target}$ target tokens rather than all positions in a single
sequence, and each $\mathbf{p}_i$ is additionally conditioned on
$\mathbf{Z}_{enc}$---the encoder's representation of the source sequence---which
enters through the cross-attention sublayers described above.

\section{Comparing the Three Architectures}

\subsection{When to Use Each Architecture}

\textbf{Encoder-Only (BERT-style)} models are the workhorse for tasks such classifying text, ranking search results for relevance to the search query, and encoding text into vector embeddings used in semantic search. \textbf{Decoder-Only (GPT-style)} models are best suited for 
open-ended generation, conversation, any task that can be framed as text completion, for example, chat bots, code generation, creative writing, and instruction following. \textbf{Encoder-Decoder (T5-style)} models are typically used for structured transformation tasks with clear input-output separation, for example, translation, and summarization.

\subsection{The Decoder-Only Dominance}

Recent trends strongly favor decoder-only architectures for large language models. The reasons include:

\textbf{Task Versatility:} With appropriate prompting, decoder-only models can perform classification, extraction, and transformation tasks by framing them as text completion.

\textbf{Scaling Efficiency:} Decoder-only models scale more predictably and achieve better performance per parameter.

\textbf{Generation Efficiency:} KV caching allows efficient generation by reusing computed key-value pairs for previous tokens.

\textbf{Simplicity:} A single architecture handles all tasks, simplifying training, deployment, and maintenance.

\textbf{In-Context Learning:} Large decoder-only models can learn new tasks from examples in the prompt without fine-tuning, a capability not anticipated but empirically observed.



\section{Architectural Innovations}

The transformer architecture described in the preceding sections has proven
remarkably stable as a foundation, but the demands of deploying models at
scale have driven a sustained wave of innovation around it. These innovations
share a common tension: every improvement to model capability tends to
increase computational cost, memory consumption, or both, while practical
deployment imposes hard constraints on all three. The subsections below trace
how the field has responded to this tension---first by making the core
mechanisms of attention more efficient, then by rethinking how expert capacity
is allocated across a model, and finally by extending the architecture to
handle multiple modalities natively. Taken together, they represent not a
departure from the transformer but a maturing of it.

% ------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Flash Attention}
% ------------------------------------------------------------

Attention, as defined in Section~\ref{sec:multi-head-attention}, requires computing the
full $N \times N$ matrix of dot products between queries and keys, where $N$
is the sequence length. The memory required to store this matrix grows
quadratically with $N$, and---crucially---the intermediate results must be
written to and read from GPU high-bandwidth memory (HBM) at every step of the
computation. For long sequences this memory traffic, not arithmetic, becomes
the dominant cost.

FlashAttention~\cite{dao2022flashattention} addressed this by recognizing that
the softmax normalization over the full row can be computed \emph{online},
without ever materializing the complete $N \times N$ matrix. As illustrated in
Figure~\ref{fig:flash_attn_algo}, the algorithm tiles the query and key-value
matrices into blocks that fit in the GPU's fast on-chip memory (SRAM),
computes attention block by block, and maintains running
statistics---the maximum logit and the log-sum-exp accumulator---that allow
the softmax to be corrected as each new block is processed. The attention
output is accumulated directly, and the large intermediate matrix is never
written to HBM. This reduces memory usage from $O(N^2)$ to $O(N)$ in the
attention layer, and cuts the number of slow memory reads and writes by a
factor proportional to the block size.

FlashAttention-2~\cite{dao2023flashattention2} refined the tiling strategy to
improve parallelism across the sequence dimension and to reduce the number of
non-matrix-multiply operations, which consume disproportionate time relative
to the highly optimised matrix multiply units available on modern hardware.

FlashAttention-3~\cite{shah2024flashattention3} introduced a further
algorithmic advance: \emph{producer-consumer asynchrony}. On hardware that
supports independent execution of data movement and matrix computation,
FlashAttention-3 assigns separate computational threads to loading the next
tile and computing attention on the current tile, so the two operations
overlap in time. It also interleaves the softmax rescaling---a relatively
cheap elementwise operation---with the subsequent block matrix multiply,
further hiding its latency. Together these changes yield roughly 1.5--2$\times$
additional speedup over FlashAttention-2 on recent hardware, while maintaining
exact numerical equivalence. Support for FP8 low-precision arithmetic, with
carefully managed quantisation error, pushes throughput higher still.

The practical impact of the FlashAttention line of work has been significant:
it enabled training and inference on sequence lengths that were previously
memory-prohibitive, directly contributing to the expansion of context windows
from a few thousand tokens to hundreds of thousands in production models.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/flash_attn.png}
    \caption{Flash Attention Algorithm. By tiling the attention computation
    into SRAM-resident blocks and maintaining online softmax statistics, the
    algorithm avoids materializing the full $N \times N$ attention matrix in
    HBM, reducing memory usage from $O(N^2)$ to $O(N)$.}
    \label{fig:flash_attn_algo}
\end{figure}

% ------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Grouped-Query Attention and Multi-Head Latent Attention}
% ------------------------------------------------------------

A separate bottleneck emerges at inference time. Autoregressive generation
requires, at each step, loading the cached key and value vectors for all
previous tokens from memory. As sequence length and batch size grow, the
bandwidth required to read this \emph{KV cache} becomes the primary limit on
generation throughput.

\textbf{Grouped-Query Attention (GQA)}~\cite{ainslie2023gqa} addresses this
by sharing key and value projections across groups of query heads. In standard
multi-head attention (MHA) with $n_h$ heads, each head maintains its own K
and V projections, so the KV cache scales as $O(n_h \cdot L)$ where $L$ is
the sequence length. Multi-query attention (MQA), as shown in
Figure~\ref{fig:mqa}, reduces this to a single shared K and V pair,
dramatically shrinking the cache but at the cost of representational capacity.
GQA interpolates: the $n_h$ query heads are divided into $G$ groups, and all
heads within a group share one K and one V projection, as shown in
Figure~\ref{fig:gqa}. The KV cache scales as $O(G \cdot L)$ rather than
$O(n_h \cdot L)$, and empirically a small number of groups (often 4--8)
recovers nearly all of MHA's quality while reducing cache size by a factor of
$n_h / G$.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/mqa.png}
    \caption{Multi Query Attention (MQA)}
    \label{fig:mqa}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/gqa.png}
    \caption{Grouped Query Attention (GQA)}
    \label{fig:gqa}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Multi-Head Latent Attention (MLA)}~\cite{deepseekai2024deepseekv2}
takes a fundamentally different approach. Rather than reducing the number of
KV heads, MLA compresses the joint key-value representation into a single
low-dimensional \emph{latent vector} per token, which is the only quantity
that needs to be cached. Full-rank keys and values are reconstructed at query
time via learned up-projection matrices.

Formally, let $\mathbf{x}_t \in \mathbb{R}^h$ be the attention input for
token $t$, where $h$ is the model's hidden dimension as defined throughout
this chapter. MLA computes a compressed latent:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{c}_t^{KV} = \mathbf{W}^{DKV} \mathbf{x}_t, \quad
\mathbf{c}_t^{KV} \in \mathbb{R}^{d_c}, \quad d_c \ll d_h n_h
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{W}^{DKV}$ is a learned down-projection, $d_h$ is the
per-head dimension, and $d_c$ is the compressed latent dimension. At query
time, keys and values are reconstructed:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{K}_t = \mathbf{W}^{UK} \mathbf{c}_t^{KV}, \qquad
\mathbf{V}_t = \mathbf{W}^{UV} \mathbf{c}_t^{KV}
\end{equation}

Only $\mathbf{c}_t^{KV}$ is stored in the cache; the up-projections
$\mathbf{W}^{UK}$ and $\mathbf{W}^{UV}$ are shared model parameters. This
reduces the KV cache footprint by a factor of approximately $d_h n_h / d_c$
compared to standard MHA---in practice more than $90\%$---while retaining the
full expressiveness of multi-head attention because the up-projections can
reconstruct distinct K and V matrices for each head. Positional information
is handled via a decoupled rotary embedding applied to a separate set of query
and key projections, so that the compressed latent itself does not depend on
absolute position and can be cached without modification.

GQA and MLA represent successive points on a design frontier: GQA reduces the
number of KV heads while keeping full-rank representations; MLA keeps a single
latent per token while recovering full-rank representations at query time. Both
are motivated by the same constraint---memory bandwidth at inference---and both
are exact rather than approximate.

% ------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Rotary Position Embeddings (RoPE)}
% ------------------------------------------------------------
 
The transformer's self-attention mechanism is permutation-invariant: it treats
its input as an unordered set and has no inherent notion of token order unless
positional information is explicitly injected. The encoder architecture in
Section~\ref{sec:encoder} introduced learned absolute position embeddings that suffer from a fundamental limitation: they assign each position an
absolute representation, so the model must learn, through training data alone,
that position 5 is close to position 6 and far from position 500. More
critically, positions beyond the training sequence length are entirely
out-of-distribution at inference time, causing sharp performance degradation
when the model encounters longer sequences than it was trained on.
 
Rotary Position Embeddings (RoPE)~\cite{su2024roformer} take a different
approach. Rather than adding a positional signal to token embeddings, RoPE
encodes position by \emph{rotating} the query and key vectors immediately
before each attention dot product. The rotation is designed so that the dot
product between a rotated query at position $m$ and a rotated key at position
$n$ depends only on the \emph{relative offset} $m - n$, not on the absolute
values of $m$ and $n$ themselves. Relative position is therefore encoded
directly in the attention score, without requiring the model to infer it from
absolute representations.
 
\subsubsection*{The Rotation Mechanism}
 
RoPE operates on pairs of dimensions. For a single pair $(x_{2i},\, x_{2i+1})$
of a query or key vector at sequence position $m$, the rotation by angle
$m\theta_i$ is the standard 2D rotation:
 
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{R}(m, \theta_i)
\begin{pmatrix} x_{2i} \\ x_{2i+1} \end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos(m\theta_i) & -\sin(m\theta_i) \\
\sin(m\theta_i) &  \cos(m\theta_i)
\end{pmatrix}
\begin{pmatrix} x_{2i} \\ x_{2i+1} \end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
 
A $d$-dimensional query or key vector is split into $d/2$ consecutive pairs,
and each pair $i$ is rotated independently by its own frequency $\theta_i$.
The full rotation matrix $\mathbf{R}_m$ acting on the entire vector is
therefore block-diagonal, with each $2 \times 2$ block being the rotation
matrix for pair $i$ at position $m$:
 
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{R}_m =
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf{R}(m,\theta_0) & & \\
& \ddots & \\
& & \mathbf{R}(m,\theta_{d/2-1})
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
 
The frequencies $\theta_i$ are \textbf{not learned}. They follow a fixed
geometric schedule parameterised by a single base constant
$\theta_{base}$ (commonly set to $10{,}000$):
 
\begin{equation}
\theta_i = \theta_{base}^{-2i/d}, \qquad i = 0, 1, \ldots, \tfrac{d}{2} - 1
\end{equation}
 
This produces a spectrum of rotation speeds. The first pair ($i=0$) rotates
fastest, completing a full revolution in $2\pi / \theta_0 = 2\pi$ positions.
The last pair ($i = d/2 - 1$) rotates extremely slowly, completing a
revolution only after $\theta_{base}^{} \cdot 2\pi$ positions. Fast-rotating
dimensions therefore encode fine-grained, short-range positional distinctions;
slow-rotating dimensions encode coarse, long-range structure. 
 
\subsubsection*{The Relative-Position Property}
 
The key algebraic consequence of rotating queries and keys by the same
block-diagonal family of matrices is that their dot product depends only on
the relative offset. For a query at position $m$ and a key at position $n$:
 
\begin{equation}
\left(\mathbf{R}_m \mathbf{q}\right)^\top
\left(\mathbf{R}_n \mathbf{k}\right)
= \mathbf{q}^\top \mathbf{R}_m^\top \mathbf{R}_n \mathbf{k}
= \mathbf{q}^\top \mathbf{R}_{n-m} \mathbf{k}
\end{equation}
 
where the last equality follows from the fact that rotation matrices compose
additively in angle: $\mathbf{R}_m^\top \mathbf{R}_n = \mathbf{R}_{n-m}$.
The result is a function of $n - m$ only. This means that two tokens
separated by an offset of 10 produce the same positional contribution to the
attention score regardless of whether they appear at positions $(1, 11)$ or
$(500, 510)$. The model therefore learns positional relationships that
generalize across absolute positions rather than memorising absolute-position
statistics from training data.
 
Because the encoding is applied to queries and keys rather than to the token
embeddings, it does not interact with the feedforward sublayers and requires
no additional parameters beyond the existing $\mathbf{W}^Q$ and $\mathbf{W}^K$
projection matrices. The rotation is applied after projection but before the
dot product, adding negligible compute.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{images/rope.png}
    \caption{Rotary Position Embeddings (RoPE). A $d$-dimensional query or
    key vector is partitioned into $d/2$ consecutive pairs. Each pair $i$ is
    rotated in its own 2D plane by angle $m\theta_i$, where $m$ is the token's
    sequence position and $\theta_i = \theta_{base}^{-2i/d}$ is a fixed
    frequency. Early pairs (small $i$, high $\theta_i$) rotate rapidly,
    encoding fine-grained local position; later pairs (large $i$, low $\theta_i$)
    rotate slowly, encoding long-range structure. Because rotation matrices
    compose additively, the dot product of a rotated query at position $m$ with
    a rotated key at position $n$ depends only on the offset $n - m$, giving
    RoPE its relative-position property without any learned positional parameters.}
    \label{fig:rope}
\end{figure}
 

 
\subsubsection*{Context Extension}
 
Although the relative-position property gives RoPE better extrapolation
behavior than absolute embeddings in principle, direct inference on sequences
much longer than the training length still degrades in practice. The root cause
is that the model's attention patterns are calibrated during training to the
rotation angles it observed; at much longer offsets, the slow-rotating
dimensions enter angular ranges they were never trained on, and the fast-rotating
dimensions begin cycling through angles that repeat at training-scale offsets,
creating ambiguity.
 
Context extension addresses this by \emph{rescaling} the rotation frequencies
at inference time. The core idea is to interpolate: rather than evaluating the
rotation at position $m$, evaluate it at position $m / s$ for a scale factor
$s > 1$, effectively compressing a longer sequence into the angular range the
model was trained on. Naive uniform rescaling of all frequencies degrades
quality because the fast-rotating dimensions, which encode short-range
structure, are most sensitive to frequency changes. More refined approaches
apply different rescaling factors to different frequency bands---rescaling the
slow-rotating dimensions more aggressively, since these are the ones that
encounter out-of-distribution angles at long range, while leaving the
fast-rotating dimensions closer to their original frequencies. This selective
rescaling has enabled models trained on sequences of $4{,}096$ tokens to
operate effectively at $128{,}000$ tokens or more without full retraining on
long-context data.
 
RoPE has become the standard positional encoding for decoder-only language
models, adopted in virtually every major open-weight model released since 2023.
Its dominance reflects the combination of three properties that learned absolute embeddings do not provide:
computational simplicity (no additional parameters, negligible overhead),
exact relative-position encoding (the relative-position property holds exactly
by construction, not approximately through training), and tractable context
extension (frequency rescaling extends the effective context length without
architectural changes).
 

% ------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Mixture of Experts (MoE)}
% ------------------------------------------------------------

A fundamental tension in language model design is that larger models
generalize better and exhibit stronger capabilities, but larger models are also
more expensive to run at inference time: every parameter is activated for every
token. Mixture of Experts (MoE) (Figure \ref{fig:mixture_of_experts})  architectures dissolve this coupling by
making the model's \emph{active} parameter count a design variable independent
of its \emph{total} parameter count.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/moe.png}
    \caption{Mixture of Experts}
    \label{fig:mixture_of_experts}
\end{figure}

The key idea is to replace the dense feedforward sublayer in each transformer
block with a set of $E$ parallel expert networks, each with the same
architecture as the original feedforward layer, together with a learned
\emph{router} that selects which experts to activate for each input token.
Only the top-$K$ experts (typically $K = 1$ or $K = 2$) are activated per
token, so the computation per token scales with $K$ rather than $E$. The
total number of parameters scales with $E$, enabling a much larger effective
model capacity for a given computational budget.

Formally, let $\mathbf{x}$ be the input to an MoE layer and let $f_i(\cdot)$
denote expert $i$. The router computes a score for each expert:

\begin{equation}
s_i = \mathbf{w}_r^\top \mathbf{x}
\end{equation}

and the top-$K$ experts are selected by their scores. The layer output is a
weighted sum over the selected experts:

\begin{equation}
\text{MoE}(\mathbf{x})
= \sum_{i \in \text{Top-}K} \frac{e^{s_i}}{\sum_{j \in \text{Top-}K} e^{s_j}} f_i(\mathbf{x})
\end{equation}

Two conceptual advances have refined this picture further. The first concerns
\textbf{expert granularity}. Standard MoE uses a small number of large
experts. An alternative is to use many small experts---increasing $E$ while
reducing each expert's hidden dimension proportionally, so that total parameter
count and per-token FLOPs remain fixed. Fine-grained experts encourage stronger
knowledge decomposition: each expert specialises in a narrower domain, and the
router can activate a more precise combination of capabilities for each token.
Alongside fine-grained routed experts, a small number of \emph{shared experts}
are activated for every token regardless of routing, handling common linguistic
patterns that appear across all contexts. This reduces redundancy among routed
experts and allows them to specialise more aggressively.

The second advance concerns \textbf{load balancing}. If the router consistently
sends most tokens to a small subset of experts---a failure mode known as routing
collapse---the computational advantage of MoE is lost: some experts are
overloaded while most sit idle. Conventional solutions add an auxiliary
loss to the training objective that penalises unequal expert utilisation.
However, this loss interferes with the primary language modelling objective and
can degrade model quality when weighted too heavily. An alternative approach
introduces a small per-expert bias term that is updated dynamically based on
observed load, without contributing to the gradient of the primary loss.
Overloaded experts receive a bias that makes them slightly less likely to be
selected; underloaded experts receive a bias in the opposite direction. Load
balance is maintained throughout training with no sacrifice to model
performance.

Together, these developments have established MoE not as an experimental
variant but as a mainstream architecture for frontier models: fine-grained
expert decomposition improves specialisation; shared experts handle common
knowledge; dynamic bias-based load balancing maintains efficiency without
quality penalties.

% ------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Multimodal Architectural Innovations}
% ------------------------------------------------------------

The sections above describe innovations that apply within a single modality.
As language models have been extended to process images, audio, and video
alongside text, new architectural questions have arisen that do not have
analogues in unimodal settings. This subsection traces the evolution of
multimodal architectures, from early approaches that bridged separately
pretrained vision and language components, to the emerging consensus around
unified tokenization that treats all modalities as first-class inputs to a
single autoregressive backbone.

\subsubsection*{The Modality Bridging Problem}

The patchification and projection described in Section~\ref{sec:vit} converts
an image into a sequence of patch embeddings in $\mathbb{R}^h$, placing it in
the same representational space as text token embeddings. This is the
simplest possible bridge: the image embeddings are concatenated with the text
token sequence and the combined sequence is processed by a standard decoder.
While conceptually clean, simple concatenation has a significant cost: the
number of visual tokens grows with image resolution. A $1024 \times 1024$
image with patch size 16 produces $4{,}096$ patch tokens, which, appended to
a text prompt, consumes a large portion of the model's context window before
any text has been processed.

This observation motivated a class of \emph{connector} architectures that
compress the visual token sequence before it reaches the language model.

\textbf{Query-based compression.} A small set of $Q$ learnable query
vectors---independent of the image---attends to the patch embeddings via
cross-attention to produce a fixed-size representation of $Q$ vectors,
regardless of image resolution:

\begin{equation}
\mathbf{V}_{compressed} = \text{CrossAttention}(\mathbf{Q}_{learned},\,
\mathbf{K}_{patches},\, \mathbf{V}_{patches})
\end{equation}

The $Q$ compressed tokens are then concatenated with the text sequence.
Setting $Q \ll N_{patches}$ decouples the number of visual tokens consumed
by the language model from image resolution, at the cost of potentially
discarding fine-grained spatial detail. 

\textbf{Cross-attention injection.} Rather than converting visual features
into tokens, visual information is injected into the language model at every
layer via additional cross-attention sublayers. At each transformer layer,
text representations form the queries; patch embeddings form the keys and
values. No visual tokens enter the text sequence at all; instead, the model
