develops its visual grounding gradually across layers. The trade-off is the opposite of query-based
compression: all spatial detail is preserved in the keys and values, but the
additional cross-attention sublayers increase per-layer compute and require
new parameters to be trained.

These approaches represent different points on a common trade-off curve
between visual fidelity, context length consumed, and parameter efficiency.
They dominated multimodal model design from 2022 through 2024.

\subsubsection*{Unified Tokenization}

The connector approaches above share a structural assumption: vision and
language are processed by separate components that are then bridged. This
assumption is increasingly being dissolved by \emph{unified tokenization},
the approach toward which the field is converging as of 2025--2026.

The central idea is to apply a \emph{discrete visual tokenizer} to images,
converting them into sequences of integer indices drawn from a learned
codebook---exactly analogous to how a text tokenizer converts a string into
a sequence of token IDs. The language model then processes a single sequence
of tokens, some drawn from the text vocabulary and some from the visual
vocabulary, under a standard next-token prediction objective.

Discretization is achieved via \emph{vector quantization}. A continuous patch
embedding $\mathbf{z} \in \mathbb{R}^h$ is mapped to the nearest entry in a
learned codebook $\mathcal{C} = \{\mathbf{e}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{e}_K\}$:

\begin{equation}
\hat{\mathbf{z}} = \mathbf{e}_{k^*}, \qquad
k^* = \arg\min_k \|\mathbf{z} - \mathbf{e}_k\|_2
\end{equation}

The codebook is learned end-to-end using a combination of a commitment loss,
which encourages the encoder output $\mathbf{z}$ to stay close to codebook
entries, and a straight-through gradient estimator, which allows gradients to
flow through the non-differentiable argmin operation during training:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{VQ} = \|\mathbf{z} - \text{sg}[\hat{\mathbf{z}}]\|_2^2
                 + \beta \|\text{sg}[\mathbf{z}] - \hat{\mathbf{z}}\|_2^2
\end{equation}

where $\text{sg}[\cdot]$ denotes the stop-gradient operator and $\beta$
is a commitment cost weighting. The codebook index $k^*$ is the visual token;
the model learns to associate these indices with semantic content through
training on large image-text corpora.

The unified vocabulary is then the union of the text vocabulary and the visual
codebook: $\mathcal{V}_{unified} = \mathcal{V}_{text} \cup \mathcal{C}$. A
mixed sequence of text and image tokens is processed autoregressively under the
standard cross-entropy loss:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L} = -\sum_t \log p(z_t \mid z_{<t})
\end{equation}

where $z_t$ may be either a text token or a visual token. The model learns
a single joint distribution over both vocabularies, enabling it to both
understand and generate image content within the same autoregressive framework.

The key advantage of unified tokenization over connector-based approaches is
architectural simplicity: there is no separate vision encoder, no connector
module, and no special-casing for visual inputs in the forward pass. The
language model backbone is unchanged; only the input representation and output
vocabulary are extended. This simplicity has practical consequences---training
stability, scaling behavior, and deployment are all governed by the same
principles as pure language models---and conceptual ones: the model treats
perceiving an image and generating an image as symmetric operations over the
same discrete token space.

The principal cost of unified tokenization is lossy compression: the argmin
quantization step is a bottleneck that discards information that does not fit
the codebook. This introduces a fidelity ceiling that does not exist in
connector-based approaches, where the patch embeddings are continuous and lossless.
Research on larger codebooks, hierarchical quantization, and residual vector
quantization is actively reducing this gap.

\subsubsection*{Multimodal Positional Encoding}

Extending positional encodings to multiple modalities introduces structure that
has no analogue in the single-modality case. In a text sequence, position is
one-dimensional: token $i$ comes before token $i+1$. In a multimodal sequence,
position has additional structure: image patches have two-dimensional spatial
coordinates, video frames have both spatial and temporal coordinates, and the
relationship between a spoken word and the video frame it accompanies is a
cross-modal temporal alignment.

\textbf{Two-dimensional RoPE.} For image patches, the rotation angles in RoPE
are computed from two position indices---row $r$ and column $c$---rather than
one. The query and key vector subspaces are partitioned into two halves: one
half is rotated according to the row position, the other according to the
column position. The resulting dot product between a query at $(r_1, c_1)$ and
a key at $(r_2, c_2)$ depends on the relative offsets $(r_1 - r_2)$ and
$(c_1 - c_2)$ independently:

\begin{equation}
\left(\mathbf{R}_{r_1,c_1}\, \mathbf{q}\right)^\top
\left(\mathbf{R}_{r_2,c_2}\, \mathbf{k}\right)
= \mathbf{q}^\top \mathbf{R}_{r_1-r_2,\,c_1-c_2}\, \mathbf{k}
\end{equation}

This preserves the relative-position property of standard RoPE while
encoding spatial layout, so the model can learn that the patch at row 5,
column 3 is spatially related to the patch at row 6, column 3 in the same
way regardless of where in the context window these patches appear.

\textbf{Temporal RoPE.} For video, a third index---frame number $f$---is
added. Each token's position triple $(r, c, f)$ generates a rotation that
factorizes across the three dimensions, so that spatial proximity within a
frame and temporal proximity across frames are encoded independently and
can both influence attention patterns.

\textbf{Cross-modal alignment.} When text, image, and audio are interleaved in
a single sequence, tokens from different modalities must be assigned position
indices that reflect their temporal alignment. A spoken word and the video
frame during which it is spoken receive the same temporal index $f$, so that
the model's relative-position encoding places them close together in the
temporal dimension regardless of their positions in the flattened token
sequence. This time-aligned positional encoding enables the model to learn
audio-visual correspondences---associating a spoken phoneme with the
corresponding lip movement, or a sound event with its visual cause---as a
natural consequence of position encoding rather than as a separately trained
alignment objective.

These extensions of RoPE to two-dimensional, temporal, and cross-modal
settings are not separate innovations but a single coherent generalization: the
rotation angle for any token is computed from its full position descriptor,
which may include spatial, temporal, and modal coordinates depending on the
application. The attention dot product then encodes relative position in all
of these dimensions simultaneously, providing the model with a rich positional
substrate on which to build multimodal representations.

\section{Conclusion}

This chapter has provided a detailed technical understanding of transformer architectures for generative AI. We've explored how encoders process sequences bidirectionally for understanding tasks, how decoders generate sequences autoregressively, and how encoder-decoders combine both for structured transformation tasks.

Several key insights emerge:

\textbf{Attention is central:} The self-attention mechanism enables transformers to capture complex dependencies and relationships in data, making them far more effective than previous architectures.

\textbf{Architecture determines capability:} The choice between encoder, decoder, and encoder-decoder architectures fundamentally shapes what tasks the model can perform and how efficiently.

\textbf{Scale matters:} Transformers benefit from scale in parameters, data, and compute, with larger models consistently outperforming smaller ones when properly trained.

\textbf{Simplicity has value:} The trend toward decoder-only architectures reflects the recognition that simpler, unified architectures can be more effective and practical than specialized designs.

\textbf{Innovation continues:} While the basic transformer architecture remains dominant, ongoing innovations in attention mechanisms, position encodings, and architectural details continue to improve efficiency and capability.

Understanding these architectural details is essential for anyone working with generative AI, whether using existing models, fine-tuning for specific applications, or developing new systems. The transformer's combination of theoretical elegance, empirical effectiveness, and practical scalability has made it the foundation of modern AI, and these architectures will likely continue to evolve while retaining their core principles of attention-based information flow.

In subsequent chapters, we'll explore how these architectures are trained on massive datasets, how they can be adapted to specific tasks and domains, and how techniques like prompt engineering and fine-tuning enable practical applications of these powerful models.

\chapter{Pretraining Models at Scale}
\label{ch:pretraining}

\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:pretraining_intro}

Training state-of-the-art generative AI models requires unprecedented
computational resources. A single large language model may contain hundreds
of billions of parameters and be trained on trillions of tokens, demanding
thousands of accelerators running for weeks or months. No single device has
sufficient memory to store such a model, let alone train it efficiently. This
chapter explores the distributed training strategies that make training at
this scale possible.

The core challenge is straightforward: modern generative AI models are too
large to fit on a single accelerator. A 70-billion parameter model requires
approximately 280 GB of memory just to store its weights in full precision,
far exceeding the capacity of any single device available today. Training
multiplies this requirement further --- activations, gradients, and optimizer
states can push memory needs four to sixteen times beyond the storage cost of
the weights alone.

The solution distributes the model, data, and computation across hundreds or
thousands of devices working in concert. This distribution introduces its own
challenges: how to partition the model and data, how to synchronize updates
across devices, how to minimize communication overhead, and how to keep
hardware utilization high. The strategies explored in this chapter --- data
parallelism, model parallelism, and various hybrid approaches --- represent
the current state of the art in distributed deep learning.

It is worth noting at the outset that the concepts in this chapter are
hardware-agnostic. Whether a training cluster is built from GPUs, TPUs, or
other AI accelerators, the same fundamental principles govern how computation,
memory, and communication are organized. Specific implementations and optimal
configurations differ by hardware and software stack, but the conceptual
framework is universal.

\section{The Scaling Challenge}
\label{sec:scaling_challenge}

\subsection{Memory Requirements}
\label{sec:memory_requirements}

To understand the necessity of distributed training, we begin by quantifying
the memory required to train a large model. Consider mixed-precision training
with the Adam optimizer, the standard configuration for large-scale runs.

\textbf{Model parameters.} Weights are stored in 16-bit floating point (FP16
or BF16, 2 bytes per parameter) for the forward and backward passes.
To maintain numerical stability during updates, a full-precision (FP32,
4 bytes) master copy is kept alongside. The total cost per parameter for
weights is therefore $2 + 4 = 6$ bytes.

\textbf{Gradients.} Gradients are computed and stored in 16-bit format,
adding $2$ bytes per parameter.

\textbf{Optimizer states.} Adam stores two FP32 quantities per parameter ---
first-moment (momentum) and second-moment (variance) estimates. These require
$4 + 4 = 8$ bytes per parameter.

The total memory per parameter during training is therefore:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:training_memory}
\text{Memory per parameter} = \underbrace{6}_{\text{weights}} +
    \underbrace{2}_{\text{gradients}} +
    \underbrace{8}_{\text{optimizer states}} = 16 \text{ bytes}
\end{equation}

For a model with $N$ parameters, total memory for model states is $16N$ bytes,
before accounting for activations. For a 175-billion parameter model, this is
$16 \times 175 \times 10^9 \approx 2.8$ TB of memory for model states alone.
Activations add further overhead proportional to batch size, sequence length,
and model depth.

Figure~\ref{fig:memory_breakdown} shows this decomposition visually. The
optimizer states account for half the memory budget, which motivates the
memory optimizations discussed in Section~\ref{sec:memory_optimizations}.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{images/memory_breakdown.png}
    \caption{Decomposition of the 16 bytes per parameter required for
    mixed-precision training with Adam. Optimizer states account for 50\%
    of the total; the FP32 master weights account for 25\%. Gradients and
    FP16 working weights together make up the remaining 25\%.}
    \label{fig:memory_breakdown}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Compute Requirements}
\label{sec:compute_requirements}

Training also demands enormous compute. The computational cost of training
a transformer is approximately:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:flops}
\text{FLOPs} \approx 6N \times D
\end{equation}

where $N$ is the number of parameters and $D$ is the number of training
tokens. The factor of 6 decomposes as approximately $2N$ FLOPs for the
forward pass and $4N$ for the backward pass (since the backward pass must
compute gradients with respect to both activations and parameters). This
estimate was established empirically in scaling law
research~\cite{kaplan2020scaling} and further validated in
subsequent work~\cite{hoffmann2022training}.

To put this in perspective: a 100-billion parameter model trained on one
trillion tokens requires approximately $6 \times 10^{23}$ floating-point
operations. On a single high-end accelerator capable of around one petaFLOP
per second of sustained throughput, this would require on the order of
seventeen years of continuous computation. Even a cluster of a thousand
such devices would take six days per training run. Large-scale training is
therefore not a luxury but a necessity.

\subsection{Scaling Laws and Compute-Optimal Training}
\label{sec:scaling_laws}

An important insight that shapes how practitioners allocate their compute
budget is the Chinchilla scaling law~\cite{hoffmann2022training}. Earlier
work~\cite{kaplan2020scaling} suggested that for a fixed compute budget,
one should prioritize larger models trained on fewer tokens. Hoffmann et al.\
showed that this was incorrect: compute-optimal training requires roughly
twenty training tokens per parameter. A model with $N$ parameters should
be trained on approximately $20N$ tokens to make full use of the compute
invested.

This result has practical consequences for distributed training. It means
that very large models must also be trained on very large datasets to justify
their parameter count, and that under-training a large model is a waste of
compute. The interaction between model size, dataset size, and compute budget
is therefore a first-order design decision before any parallelization strategy
is chosen.

% ============================================================
% PATCH: Chapter 5 replacement sections
% Replaces \section{Collective Communication Operations}
% through end of \section{Combining Parallelization Strategies}
% ============================================================

\section{Collective Communication Operations}
\label{sec:collective_comm}

Distributed training relies on collective communication primitives that
coordinate information across devices. These operations are the building
blocks from which all parallelization strategies are constructed. Before
examining how each parallelization strategy works, it is worth understanding
exactly what these primitives do, since they appear repeatedly throughout
the chapter.

\subsection{All-Reduce}
\label{sec:all_reduce}

All-Reduce combines values from all devices using a reduction operation ---
typically summation --- and distributes the result back to every device.
After an All-Reduce, every device holds the same reduced value.

Figure~\ref{fig:all_reduce} illustrates this operation. In data parallelism,
All-Reduce synchronizes gradients: each device computes gradients on its
local mini-batch, All-Reduce sums them across devices, and every device then
holds the globally summed gradient and divides by the number of devices to
obtain the average. All devices then perform identical parameter updates,
keeping all replicas synchronized.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{images/all_reduce.png}
    \caption{All-Reduce with four devices. Before the operation, each device
    holds a distinct value. After the operation, every device holds the sum
    of all values. This is used in data parallelism to synchronize gradients
    across all devices.}
    \label{fig:all_reduce}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Reduce-Scatter}
\label{sec:reduce_scatter}

Reduce-Scatter performs a reduction but distributes different portions of the result to different devices. Each device starts with a full vector; after Reduce-Scatter, device $i$ holds only the $i$-th portion of the element-wise sum across all devices.

Figure~\ref{fig:reduce_scatter} illustrates this. In FSDP, Reduce-Scatter
is used after backpropagation: each device contributes its locally computed
gradient vector, the operation sums them elementwise and partitions the
result so that each device receives exactly the gradient shard corresponding
to its parameter shard.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{images/reduce_scatter.png}
    \caption{Reduce-Scatter with four devices. Each device begins with a
    4-element vector. After the operation, each device holds one element ---
    the sum of the corresponding elements across all devices. Used in FSDP
    to distribute reduced gradients across devices.}
    \label{fig:reduce_scatter}
\end{figure}

\subsection{All-Gather}
\label{sec:all_gather}

All-Gather is the complement of Reduce-Scatter. Each device holds one shard
of a tensor; after All-Gather, every device holds the complete concatenated
tensor.

Figure~\ref{fig:all_gather} illustrates this. In FSDP, All-Gather
reconstructs full parameter tensors from their shards before each forward
or backward pass. The reconstructed parameters are discarded immediately
after use --- each device retains only its own shard --- so that memory
stays at $\frac{1}{n}$ of the full model between compute steps.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{images/all_gather.png}
    \caption{All-Gather with four devices. Each device begins with one shard
    of a distributed tensor. After the operation, every device holds the
    complete concatenated tensor. Used in FSDP to reconstruct full parameters
    from shards before forward and backward passes.}
    \label{fig:all_gather}
\end{figure}


\subsection{Communication Costs}
\label{sec:comm_costs}

For $n$ devices and a tensor of total size $m$ elements, the per-device
bandwidth required under a ring algorithm is approximately
$\frac{m(n-1)}{n}$ for both Reduce-Scatter and All-Gather, and
$\frac{2m(n-1)}{n}$ for All-Reduce (which decomposes into one Reduce-Scatter
followed by one All-Gather). As $n$ grows large, all three approach $m$ per
device: communication cost does not grow with the number of devices for
a fixed tensor size. However, when tensor size itself scales with the model
--- as it does in large language models --- communication can become the
dominant cost, making the choice of parallelization strategy and network
topology critical.

% ============================================================
% PATCH: ZeRO section — restore memory arithmetic examples
% ============================================================

\section{Zero Redundancy Optimizer}
\label{sec:zero}

The Zero Redundancy Optimizer (ZeRO)~\cite{rajbhandari2020zero} eliminates
memory redundancy in data parallelism while maintaining its computational
efficiency. The central insight is that devices do not need to hold redundant
copies of every training state: each device needs the full parameters during
forward and backward passes, but optimizer states and gradients are only
needed locally during the update step. ZeRO exploits this by partitioning
states across devices and reconstructing them on demand.

\subsection{ZeRO Stages}
\label{sec:zero_stages}

ZeRO proceeds in three stages of increasing aggressiveness, each eliminating
a different source of redundancy. Figure~\ref{fig:zero_stages} summarizes
the memory per device at each stage and Figure~\ref{fig:zero_memory} shows
the reduction for a concrete example.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/zero_stages.png}
    \caption{Per-device memory under DDP and each ZeRO stage for a 175B
    parameter model on 1000 devices. Each bar is decomposed into parameter,
    gradient, and optimizer state contributions. ZeRO Stage 3 reduces
    per-device memory from 2.8 TB to 2.8 GB --- a factor of 1000.}
    \label{fig:zero_stages}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Stage 1 --- Optimizer State Partitioning.} Optimizer states (8 bytes
per parameter in Adam) are partitioned equally across all $n$ devices. Each
device stores only $\frac{1}{n}$ of the optimizer states while keeping full
copies of parameters (6 bytes) and gradients (2 bytes). Memory per device
becomes:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:zero1}
\text{ZeRO-1 memory} = 6N + 2N + \frac{8N}{n} = 8N + \frac{8N}{n}
\end{equation}

For $n = 1000$ devices this is effectively $8N$ bytes per device --- a $2\times$
reduction from the $16N$ baseline --- with minimal additional communication:
each device simply updates its optimizer state shard after the standard
All-Reduce.

\textbf{Stage 2 --- Gradient Partitioning.} Additionally, gradients (2 bytes
per parameter) are partitioned. A Reduce-Scatter replaces the All-Reduce for
gradients, so each device accumulates only its assigned gradient shard.
Memory per device becomes:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:zero2}
\text{ZeRO-2 memory} = 6N + \frac{2N}{n} + \frac{8N}{n} = 6N + \frac{10N}{n}
\end{equation}

For $n = 1000$ this is effectively $6N$ bytes per device --- a $2.67\times$
reduction from the baseline.

\textbf{Stage 3 --- Parameter Partitioning.} Additionally, parameters are
partitioned. Each device stores only $\frac{1}{n}$ of the parameters,
gradients, and optimizer states. Memory per device becomes:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:zero3}
\text{ZeRO-3 memory} = \frac{6N}{n} + \frac{2N}{n} + \frac{8N}{n}
    = \frac{16N}{n}
\end{equation}

This is a factor-of-$n$ reduction from DDP. For a 175-billion parameter
model ($N = 175 \times 10^9$) on $n = 1000$ devices:


\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/zero_memory.png}
    \caption{Per-device memory under DDP and each ZeRO stage for a 175B
    parameter model on 1000 devices. Each bar is decomposed into parameter,
    gradient, and optimizer state contributions. ZeRO Stage 3 reduces
    per-device memory from 2.8 TB to 2.8 GB --- a factor of 1000.}
    \label{fig:zero_memory}
\end{figure}


\subsection{Fully Sharded Data Parallel}
\label{sec:fsdp}

Fully Sharded Data Parallel (FSDP)~\cite{zhao2023pytorch} implements ZeRO
Stage 3 with a layer-by-layer protocol designed to minimize peak memory.
Figure~\ref{fig:fsdp_protocol} shows the sequence of operations for a
two-layer model on four devices.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/fsdp_protocol.png}
    \caption{FSDP forward and backward pass protocol for a two-layer model
    on four devices. Each device holds only its parameter shard between
    compute steps. Parameters are reconstructed via All-Gather immediately
    before each layer computation and discarded immediately after. Gradients
    are distributed via Reduce-Scatter after each backward step.}
    \label{fig:fsdp_protocol}
\end{figure}

The forward pass for each layer $\ell$ proceeds as follows:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{All-Gather}: Collect the sharded parameters for layer $\ell$
    from all devices, reconstructing the full layer weights on every device.
\item \textbf{Compute}: Perform the forward pass through layer $\ell$ using
    the reconstructed weights.
\item \textbf{Discard}: Free the gathered parameters for layer $\ell$; each
    device retains only its own shard.
\item Advance to layer $\ell + 1$ and repeat.
\end{enumerate}

The backward pass mirrors this in reverse for each layer $\ell$:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{All-Gather}: Reconstruct the full parameters for layer $\ell$.
\item \textbf{Compute}: Perform the backward pass through layer $\ell$,
    computing gradients with respect to parameters and inputs.
\item \textbf{Reduce-Scatter}: Sum gradients across devices and distribute
    shards so each device accumulates only the gradient shard corresponding
    to its parameter shard.
\item \textbf{Update}: Each device updates its parameter shard using its
    gradient shard and optimizer state shard. No further communication is
    required for this step.
\item \textbf{Discard}: Free the gathered parameters for layer $\ell$.
\item Advance to layer $\ell - 1$ and repeat.
\end{enumerate}

At any point outside the brief All-Gather window, each device holds only
$\frac{16N}{n}$ bytes of model state. The temporary full-layer parameters
during a forward or backward step cover only one layer at a time, which is
much smaller than the full model. For a model with $L$ layers, FSDP performs
$2L$ All-Gather operations and $L$ Reduce-Scatter operations per training
step, compared to a single All-Reduce in DDP. This increased communication
cost is the price of the factor-of-$n$ memory reduction.

\section{Model Parallelism}
\label{sec:model_parallelism}
 
Data parallelism, even with full sharding, still requires each device to
compute full forward and backward passes through the model. When individual
layers are too large for a single device, or when the communication overhead
of FSDP becomes prohibitive, we must partition the model itself. Model
parallelism splits the model across devices so that each device is
responsible for only a portion of the computation.
 
\subsection{Tensor Parallelism}
\label{sec:tensor_parallelism}
 
Tensor parallelism splits individual layers across devices, partitioning
the weight matrices themselves. Consider the MLP layer with input
$\mathbf{X} \in \mathbb{R}^{b \times h}$ and weight matrix
$\mathbf{W}^1 \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times 4h}$:
 
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:mlp_layer}
\mathbf{Y} = \text{GeLU}(\mathbf{X}\mathbf{W}^1)
\end{equation}
 
With 2-way tensor parallelism, we partition $\mathbf{W}^1$ column-wise:
 
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:tp_split}
\mathbf{W}^1 = [\mathbf{W}^1_A \mid \mathbf{W}^1_B]
\end{equation}
 
Each device independently computes its half of the output --- Device 1
computes $\mathbf{Y}_A = \text{GeLU}(\mathbf{X}\mathbf{W}^1_A)$ and Device 2
computes $\mathbf{Y}_B = \text{GeLU}(\mathbf{X}\mathbf{W}^1_B)$ --- and an
All-Reduce then combines contributions for the subsequent layer
(Figure~\ref{fig:tp_mlp_layer}). Both devices must hold the full input
$\mathbf{X}$, which is broadcast or already replicated.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/tp_mlp_layer.png}
    \caption{Tensor parallelism for an MLP layer. The weight matrix is
    partitioned column-wise across two devices. Each device computes its
    portion of the output independently. An All-Reduce synchronizes the
    results before the next layer.}
    \label{fig:tp_mlp_layer}
\end{figure}
 
Multi-head attention is particularly amenable to tensor parallelism because
attention heads are independent by construction. With $H$ heads and $d$
devices, each device computes $H/d$ heads in full:
 
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:tp_attention}
\text{head}_{i,j} = \text{Attention}(\mathbf{Q}_{i,j},\, \mathbf{K}_{i,j},\,
\mathbf{V}_{i,j}), \quad j = 1, \ldots, H/d
\end{equation}
 
The outputs are concatenated via All-Reduce before the output projection
(Figure~\ref{fig:tp_attn_layer}).
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/tp_attn_layer.png}
    \caption{Tensor parallelism for multi-head attention. Each device
    computes a disjoint subset of attention heads. An All-Reduce combines
    the outputs before the projection layer.}
    \label{fig:tp_attn_layer}
\end{figure}
 
Tensor parallelism requires one All-Reduce per layer per forward pass and per
backward pass, meaning it generates frequent fine-grained communication.
It is therefore practical only when devices are connected by very high
bandwidth interconnects --- typically within a single physical node. Across
nodes, the latency and bandwidth constraints make tensor parallelism
prohibitively expensive.
 
\subsection{Pipeline Parallelism}
\label{sec:pipeline_parallelism}
 
Pipeline parallelism partitions the model layer-wise across devices: different
devices hold different layers, and activations flow sequentially from one
device to the next (Figure~\ref{fig:pp}). With a 12-layer model and 3 devices,
Device 1 holds layers 1--4, Device 2 holds layers 5--8, and Device 3 holds
layers 9--12.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/pp.png}
    \caption{Pipeline parallelism assigns different layers to different
    devices. Activations flow left to right during the forward pass;
    gradients flow right to left during the backward pass.}
    \label{fig:pp}
\end{figure}
 
The fundamental problem with naive pipelining is the \emph{pipeline bubble}:
while Device 1 is processing a batch, Devices 2 and 3 are idle waiting for
activations. Only one device is active at a time, which wastes most of the
cluster.
 
The solution is micro-batching: split each mini-batch into $m$ micro-batches
and feed them through the pipeline in sequence. While Device 2 is processing
micro-batch 1, Device 1 is already working on micro-batch 2. This overlaps
computation across stages and dramatically reduces idle time. The remaining
bubble fraction --- the fraction of time each device spends idle despite
micro-batching --- is approximately:
 
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:bubble}
\text{Bubble fraction} \approx \frac{p - 1}{m}
\end{equation}
 
where $p$ is the number of pipeline stages and $m$ is the number of
micro-batches. With $p = 4$ stages and $m = 32$ micro-batches, the bubble
fraction is under 10\%. The cost is increased memory for storing activations
across micro-batches and the complexity of scheduling forward and backward
passes across stages.
 
Unlike tensor parallelism, pipeline parallelism requires only point-to-point
communication between adjacent pipeline stages --- passing activations
forward and gradients backward --- and is tolerant of moderate inter-node
bandwidth. It is therefore the preferred strategy for parallelism that
crosses node boundaries.
 
\subsection{Sequence Parallelism}
\label{sec:sequence_parallelism}
 
Sequence parallelism partitions the sequence dimension across devices
(Figure~\ref{fig:sp}). For a sequence of length $s$ with $d$ devices,
Device $k$ processes tokens $\frac{(k-1)s}{d} + 1$ through $\frac{ks}{d}$.
This reduces activation memory proportionally to $d$, which is valuable
for long-context models where activation memory can exceed parameter memory.
 
\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{images/sp.png}
    \caption{Sequence parallelism for an MLP layer. The sequence dimension
    is partitioned across devices, with each device processing a contiguous
    slice of the token sequence.}
    \label{fig:sp}
\end{figure}
 
Operations that are purely position-wise (such as the MLP and layer
normalization) require no communication under sequence parallelism. Operations
that require information across positions --- most importantly, full attention
--- require All-Gather and Reduce-Scatter operations to exchange key and value
representations. Sequence parallelism is therefore most naturally combined
with architectures or attention patterns that limit cross-sequence
dependencies, such as sliding-window attention or ring attention.

% ============================================================
% PATCH: 3D Parallelism section — restore worked arithmetic
% ============================================================

\section{Combining Parallelization Strategies}
\label{sec:3d_parallelism}

No single parallelization strategy suffices for frontier model training.
Tensor parallelism is limited to high-bandwidth within-node communication.
Pipeline parallelism introduces bubbles. FSDP increases communication volume.
Each strategy makes a different trade-off between memory, communication, and
compute efficiency. The standard solution is to combine them in a hierarchy
that matches the communication characteristics of each strategy to the
network topology of the cluster.

A typical configuration uses three levels of parallelism simultaneously,
often called 3D parallelism (Figure~\ref{fig:3d_parallelism}):

\textbf{Tensor parallelism within each node.} High-bandwidth intra-node
interconnects support the fine-grained, frequent All-Reduce operations that
tensor parallelism requires. A degree of 8 is common for an 8-device node.

\textbf{Pipeline parallelism across nodes.} Point-to-point activation
passing between pipeline stages requires only moderate bandwidth and
tolerates higher latency, making it well matched to inter-node network links.
A degree of 4 to 8 pipeline stages is typical.

\textbf{Data parallelism (FSDP) across pipeline replicas.} Multiple copies
of the full pipeline run in parallel on different data shards. Each replica
communicates gradients via Reduce-Scatter and parameters via All-Gather.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/3d_parallelism.png}
    \caption{3D parallelism organizes devices into a three-dimensional
    hierarchy. Tensor parallelism groups devices within each node (inner
    dimension). Pipeline parallelism chains nodes sequentially (depth
    dimension). Data parallelism replicates the full pipeline across
    independent data shards (outer dimension).}
    \label{fig:3d_parallelism}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Worked example.} Consider training a 175-billion parameter model on
1024 devices. Figure~\ref{fig:3d_example} traces the parameter reduction
at each level of the hierarchy.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/3d_example.png}
    \caption{Step-by-step parameter reduction through 3D parallelism for a
    175B parameter model on 1024 devices. Each level of the hierarchy reduces
    the parameters each device must work with, from the full 175B at the
    start to approximately 170M stored per device at the end.}
    \label{fig:3d_example}
\end{figure}

We begin with the full 175-billion parameter model. Applying 8-way tensor
parallelism within each node reduces the parameter footprint per device to
$\frac{175\text{B}}{8} \approx 22\text{B}$ parameters. Applying 4-way
pipeline parallelism distributes layers across 4 nodes so each pipeline
stage holds $\frac{22\text{B}}{4} \approx 5.5\text{B}$ parameters.
Finally, 32-way FSDP shards those parameters across 32 data-parallel
replicas, so each device stores only $\frac{5.5\text{B}}{32} \approx 170\text{M}$
parameters at any given time. The total device count is
$8 \times 4 \times 32 = 1024$, and a 175-billion parameter model that
required 2.8 TB of model-state memory is now tractable on devices with
tens of gigabytes of memory each.

The configuration must be tuned jointly: tensor parallelism degree is
constrained by intra-node bandwidth; pipeline parallelism degree is
constrained by inter-node bandwidth and the acceptable bubble fraction (Equation~\ref{eq:bubble}.

\section{Memory Optimizations}
\label{sec:memory_optimizations}

Parallelization strategies reduce memory by distributing the model across
devices. A complementary set of techniques reduces the memory each device
needs in the first place.

\subsection{Mixed Precision and Numerical Formats}
\label{sec:mixed_precision}

Mixed-precision training uses lower-precision formats for computationally
intensive operations while retaining full precision where numerical stability
requires it. The standard configuration keeps forward and backward pass
computations in 16-bit format while maintaining FP32 master weights and
performing optimizer updates in FP32.

Two 16-bit formats are in common use. FP16 offers higher mantissa precision
but a narrow dynamic range, making it susceptible to overflow and underflow
in deep networks. BF16 shares the same 8-bit exponent as FP32, giving it
the same dynamic range and making it more robust for training; it has become
the default for large-scale runs. More recently, 8-bit floating-point (FP8)
formats have entered production use, halving the memory footprint and
doubling the theoretical throughput of FP16/BF16 for covered operations.
FP8 comes in two variants suited to different roles: E4M3 (4 exponent bits,
3 mantissa bits) is used for weights and activations, where dynamic range
matters less; E5M2 (5 exponent bits, 2 mantissa bits) is used for gradients,
which span a wider dynamic range. FP8 training requires per-tensor scaling to
compensate for the narrow representable range, adding implementation
complexity, but the memory and throughput gains are substantial enough that
frontier training runs increasingly use it for the bulk of computation.

The general principle is the same across all these formats: match precision
to the numerical sensitivity of each operation. Optimizer states require
FP32 because small weight updates would be lost in lower precision. Matrix
multiplications in the forward pass tolerate FP16 or BF16 without
measurable loss in model quality. The most compute-intensive operations are
therefore performed in the lowest precision that maintains training stability.

\subsection{Gradient Checkpointing}
\label{sec:gradient_checkpointing}

During a standard forward pass, all intermediate activations are stored for
use in the backward pass. For a model with $L$ layers, this requires $O(L)$
activation memory, which can be larger than the parameter memory for large
batch sizes or long sequences.

Gradient checkpointing trades compute for memory by storing activations only
at selected \emph{checkpoint} boundaries rather than after every layer.
During the backward pass, intermediate activations between checkpoints are
recomputed from the nearest stored checkpoint rather than retrieved from
memory. Checkpointing every $k$ layers reduces activation memory from $O(L)$
to $O(L/k)$ at the cost of one additional forward pass per checkpoint
interval, typically adding around 30\% to total compute. In practice,
checkpointing every 3--5 layers reduces activation memory by 3--5$\times$
with acceptable overhead, and for very long sequences it is often the
difference between a batch fitting in memory or not.

\subsection{Gradient Clipping}
\label{sec:gradient_clipping}

Large-scale training is susceptible to gradient explosions --- occasional
abnormally large gradient updates that destabilize training and can be
difficult to recover from. Gradient clipping addresses this by rescaling
the gradient vector when its norm exceeds a threshold $\tau$:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:grad_clip}
g \leftarrow g \cdot \min\!\left(1,\, \frac{\tau}{\|g\|}\right)
\end{equation}

This preserves the direction of the gradient while bounding its magnitude.
Values of $\tau$ between 0.5 and 1.0 are typical for large transformer
training. Gradient clipping is applied after gradient synchronization across
devices, so it operates on the globally averaged gradient rather than local
per-device gradients.

\section{Practical Considerations}
\label{sec:practical}

\subsection{Choosing a Parallelization Strategy}
\label{sec:choosing_parallelism}

The choice of parallelization strategy depends primarily on model size and
available hardware. For models below roughly 10 billion parameters, DDP or
FSDP alone is typically sufficient: the model fits within the combined memory
of a modest cluster, and the simplicity of pure data parallelism is a
significant advantage. Mixed precision and gradient checkpointing should be
enabled regardless.

For models in the 10--100 billion parameter range, FSDP handles memory
reduction and tensor parallelism within nodes can provide additional capacity
if needed. The communication overhead of FSDP becomes significant at this
scale, so overlapping All-Gather operations with computation (prefetching
the next layer's parameters during the current layer's computation) becomes
important for efficiency.

For frontier models above 100 billion parameters, full 3D parallelism is
generally necessary. The configuration must be tuned jointly: tensor
parallelism degree is constrained by intra-node bandwidth; pipeline parallelism
degree is constrained by inter-node bandwidth and the acceptable bubble
fraction; data parallelism degree fills the remaining devices. Careful
load balancing across pipeline stages --- ensuring each stage has roughly
equal computational cost --- is critical for avoiding stragglers.

\subsection{Training Efficiency}
\label{sec:mfu}

The standard metric for large-scale training efficiency is Model FLOPs
Utilization (MFU): the fraction of the hardware's theoretical peak
throughput actually used for model computation, excluding recomputation
overhead:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:mfu}
\text{MFU} = \frac{\text{Observed FLOPs/sec}}{\text{Peak FLOPs/sec}}
\end{equation}

Well-optimized large-scale training typically achieves 40--60\%
MFU~\cite{chowdhery2023palm}. The gap from 100\% reflects communication
overhead, pipeline bubbles, memory bandwidth constraints, and load imbalance.
Improving MFU by a few percentage points on a large cluster translates
directly to reduced training time and cost, making it a key optimization
target.

\section{Conclusion}
\label{sec:pretraining_conclusion}

This chapter has developed the engineering foundations that make large-scale
generative AI training possible. The central tension throughout is between
memory and communication: every technique that reduces memory per device ---
FSDP, tensor parallelism, mixed precision, gradient checkpointing --- does
so either by distributing data across devices, which increases communication,
or by recomputing rather than storing, which increases compute. There is no
free lunch, and the right configuration is always a trade-off calibrated
to the specific cluster topology, model architecture, and time budget.

Three principles organize the choices. First, match parallelism granularity
to interconnect bandwidth: fine-grained tensor parallelism belongs within
high-bandwidth nodes, coarser pipeline parallelism belongs across nodes.
Second, eliminate redundancy before adding complexity: ZeRO/FSDP should
be the baseline before introducing model parallelism. Third, use the lowest
precision that maintains training stability: the transition from FP32 to
BF16 to FP8 follows the same logic at each step.

Chapter~6 builds on these foundations by examining what happens after
pretraining: how models are fine-tuned on specific tasks, aligned with
human preferences, and adapted to deployment constraints that are the
inverse of the training-time concerns developed here --- where the challenge
shifts from fitting the model in a cluster to fitting it on a single
inference device.

\chapter{Adapting Pre-trained Models}

\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:adapt_intro}

A pre-trained generative model encodes an enormous amount of general
knowledge --- language structure, reasoning patterns, factual associations,
stylistic conventions --- acquired from training on vast and diverse data.
This generality is the source of its value: the same model can answer
questions, write code, summarize documents, and translate between languages.
It is also the source of its limitation: a model trained to approximate a
broad distribution rarely excels at any particular corner of it.

Adapting a pre-trained model to a specific task, domain, or use case means
moving it from general competence toward specialized excellence. Every
technique explored in this chapter is a way of doing that --- and every
technique confronts the same fundamental tension. The knowledge that makes
the model useful for the target task is entangled with the knowledge that
makes it useful for everything else. Push the model too hard toward
specialization and it loses the generality that made it worth adapting in
the first place. This phenomenon --- \emph{catastrophic forgetting} --- is
not a bug to be patched but a structural constraint that shapes every
adaptation decision. Understanding it is the thread that runs through the
entire chapter.

The techniques available fall into two families. The first adapts the
\emph{input}: prompt engineering and retrieval-augmented generation guide
the model toward desired behavior by changing what it is shown, leaving its
parameters entirely untouched. Because no parameters change, catastrophic
forgetting is impossible --- but so is teaching the model genuinely new
behaviors or deeply internalizing domain knowledge. The second family adapts
the \emph{model}: fine-tuning updates parameters through additional training
on task-specific data, trading some degree of generality for specialized
capability. The risk of forgetting is real but manageable, particularly
with parameter-efficient methods that constrain how much the parameters can
change.

The chapter proceeds as follows. Section~\ref{sec:prompt_engineering} covers
prompt engineering --- zero-shot, few-shot, and chain-of-thought approaches.
Section~\ref{sec:rag} covers retrieval-augmented generation. Together these
represent the input-adaptation family. Section~\ref{sec:sft} covers supervised
fine-tuning, and Section~\ref{sec:peft} covers parameter-efficient fine-tuning,
centering on LoRA as the most theoretically transparent and practically
dominant method. Section~\ref{sec:forgetting} addresses catastrophic
forgetting directly, since it bears on the design of any fine-tuning
approach. Section~\ref{sec:adaptation_practice} closes with practical
guidance on combining these techniques. Alignment training --- the
specialization of fine-tuning concerned with making models safe, honest,
and helpful rather than merely capable --- is the subject of
Chapter~\ref{ch:rlhf}.

\section{Prompt Engineering: Adapting the Input}
\label{sec:prompt_engineering}

Prompt engineering is the practice of crafting inputs that elicit desired
behaviors from a model without modifying its parameters. Despite its
apparent simplicity, effective prompt engineering requires understanding
how models process language, what patterns they have internalized during
pre-training, and how to structure information to guide generation.

\subsection{Zero-Shot Prompting}
\label{sec:zero_shot}

Zero-shot prompting presents a task to the model with instructions but no
examples. The model must understand and execute the task based solely on
the instruction and its pre-trained knowledge.

\textbf{Example.}
\begin{verbatim}
Tell me the sentiment of the following social media post and
categorize it as positive, negative, or neutral:

"Don't miss the electric vehicle revolution! This company is
ditching muscle cars for EVs, creating a huge opportunity
for investors."
\end{verbatim}

A capable model correctly identifies positive sentiment based on phrases
like ``huge opportunity'' and the enthusiastic tone, without any examples
of sentiment classification.

Zero-shot prompting works well when the task is common enough to have been
well-represented in pre-training data, when the model is large enough to
generalize from instructions alone, and when the model has been instruction
fine-tuned (covered in Section~\ref{sec:instruction_finetuning}). It tends
to fail when the task is genuinely novel, when the required output format
is complex or non-standard, or when fine-grained distinctions are needed
that are difficult to specify in natural language alone. Several practices
consistently improve zero-shot performance: stating the output format
explicitly (``respond with a single word: Positive, Negative, or Neutral''),
providing relevant context or a role framing, decomposing complex tasks
into sequential sub-tasks, and specifying constraints on what the model
should not do. When zero-shot performance is insufficient, the natural
next step is few-shot prompting.

\subsection{Few-Shot Prompting}
\label{sec:few_shot}

Few-shot prompting provides the model with a small number of
input-output examples --- typically one to ten --- before presenting the
actual query. The model infers the task structure from the examples and
applies it to the new input.

\textbf{Example.}
\begin{verbatim}
Classify the sentiment of the following headlines.

Research firm fends off allegations of impropriety over new
technology.
Answer: Negative

Offshore windfarms continue to thrive as vocal minority in
opposition dwindles.
Answer: Positive

Manufacturing plant is the latest target in investigation by
state officials.
Answer:
\end{verbatim}

The model correctly identifies negative sentiment by learning from the
pattern established in the examples, particularly that ``target in
investigation'' carries similar negative connotation to ``allegations''
in the first example.

Several principles govern effective few-shot prompting. Example quality
matters more than quantity: a few clear, representative examples outperform
many noisy ones. The examples should cover different sub-cases within the
task, including edge cases, to improve generalization. Format consistency
is critical --- the model learns the output format as much as the task
itself, so every example must follow identical formatting. Interestingly,
research has shown that few-shot learning is remarkably robust to label
noise: incorrect labels in examples often still improve performance over
zero-shot prompting, because the model learns the task structure even from
imperfect demonstrations.

One to three examples often suffice for simple tasks or capable models.
Three to five is a reasonable default for most classification and extraction
tasks. Beyond ten examples, returns diminish sharply, and the practical
constraint of the model's context window begins to bind. When you find
yourself wanting many more examples than the context window allows, this
is a strong signal to consider fine-tuning instead.

\subsection{Chain-of-Thought Prompting}
\label{sec:cot}

Chain-of-thought prompting encourages models to generate intermediate
reasoning steps before producing a final answer. This technique
substantially improves performance on tasks requiring multi-step reasoning,
arithmetic, or logical inference.

The key insight is that autoregressive generation is conditioned on all
previously generated tokens. By generating reasoning steps explicitly, the
model creates a scratchpad of intermediate results that subsequent generation
can depend on --- effectively extending working memory beyond what a single
forward pass can maintain implicitly. This allows the model to break complex
problems into sub-problems, maintain intermediate results, and catch
inconsistencies during reasoning.

Two variants exist. \emph{Zero-shot chain-of-thought} simply appends
``Think step by step'' or equivalent instruction to the prompt. This alone
is often sufficient for mathematical reasoning and logical inference.
\emph{Few-shot chain-of-thought} provides examples that include explicit
reasoning steps, teaching the model both what to do and how to structure
the reasoning.

\textbf{Example (zero-shot CoT).}
\begin{verbatim}
Which vehicle requires a larger down payment?

Vehicle A costs $40,000 with a 30% down payment required.
Vehicle B costs $50,000 with a 20% down payment required.

Think step by step.
\end{verbatim}

Without the chain-of-thought instruction, models frequently attempt to
answer directly and make errors in mental arithmetic. With it, the model
computes each down payment explicitly (Vehicle A: \$12,000; Vehicle B:
\$10,000) and arrives at the correct comparison. The trade-off is increased
latency and token cost --- the reasoning steps consume context and take
time to generate. Use chain-of-thought selectively for tasks where
reasoning quality matters more than speed.

\subsection{Best Practices}
\label{sec:prompting_best_practices}

Across all prompting approaches, several principles consistently improve
results. State the task explicitly and define terms that might be
ambiguous. Specify exactly what should appear in the output, including
format, length, and what to avoid. Provide relevant background and set
an appropriate tone. Test prompts on diverse examples that include edge
cases, not just representative ones, and iterate based on failure modes
rather than successes. Treat prompts as code: version them, test changes
before deployment, and monitor performance over time.

Prompt security deserves particular attention in production systems.
Prompt injection --- where user-provided content contains instructions
that override the system prompt --- is a real attack vector. Clearly
separate instructions from user-provided data using delimiters, validate
inputs before including them in prompts, and design prompts with the
assumption that user input may be adversarial.

\section{Retrieval-Augmented Generation}
\label{sec:rag}

Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG)~\cite{lewis2020retrieval} addresses
a fundamental limitation of pre-trained models: their knowledge is frozen
at the time of training. RAG systems dynamically retrieve relevant
information from external sources and include it in the prompt, enabling
models to generate responses grounded in current, accurate, and often
proprietary data --- without modifying any model parameters.

\subsection{Architecture}
\label{sec:rag_architecture}

A RAG system has three components. The \emph{retriever} searches an
external knowledge base for documents relevant to the user's query. The
\emph{augmenter} combines retrieved documents with the query into a single
prompt. The \emph{generator} --- the pre-trained model --- produces a
response conditioned on this augmented prompt.
Figure~\ref{fig:rag_architecture} shows this pipeline.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/rag_architecture.png}
    \caption{The RAG pipeline. A user query is used to retrieve relevant
    documents from an external knowledge base. The retrieved documents are
    combined with the query into an augmented prompt, which the language
    model uses to generate a grounded response. The knowledge base can be
    updated independently of the model, enabling access to current
    information without retraining.}
    \label{fig:rag_architecture}
\end{figure}

The system operates through two workflows. The \emph{ingestion workflow}
runs offline: documents are collected, preprocessed, chunked into segments
of typically 300--500 tokens, and indexed for retrieval. The \emph{query
workflow} runs online for each user request: the query is used to retrieve
relevant chunks, which are inserted into the prompt alongside the query,
and the model generates a response. This architecture enables access to
information updated after training, proprietary knowledge absent from
training data, and source attribution that grounds generated claims in
specific documents.

\subsection{Retrieval Strategies}
\label{sec:retrieval_strategies}

The retriever is the most consequential component of a RAG system ---
generating the right information determines whether the model can answer
correctly. Three retrieval strategies exist, each with distinct trade-offs.
Figure~\ref{fig:retrieval_comparison} compares them.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.90\textwidth]{images/retrieval_comparison.png}
    \caption{Three retrieval strategies for RAG. Syntactic (lexical)
    retrieval matches query and document vocabulary using term statistics;
    it is fast and precise for exact matches but fails when query and
    document use different words for the same concept. Semantic retrieval
    encodes meaning as dense vectors and matches by cosine similarity;
    it handles paraphrases and synonyms but requires embedding
    infrastructure. Hybrid retrieval combines both, fusing ranked lists
    via reciprocal rank fusion to capture the strengths of each approach.}
    \label{fig:retrieval_comparison}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Syntactic retrieval} finds documents containing specific words or
phrases from the query. The industry standard is BM25~\cite{robertson2009probabilistic},
which scores documents by term frequency with saturation and document-length
normalization:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:bm25}
\text{BM25}(q, d) = \sum_{t \in q} \text{IDF}(t) \cdot
    \frac{\text{TF}(t, d) \cdot (k_1 + 1)}
         {\text{TF}(t, d) + k_1 \cdot \left(1 - b + b \cdot
         \frac{|d|}{\text{avgdl}}\right)}
\end{equation}

where $k_1 \in [1.2, 2.0]$ controls term frequency saturation, $b \approx
0.75$ controls length normalization, $|d|$ is document length, and
$\text{avgdl}$ is the mean document length in the corpus. BM25 is fast
(millisecond search over millions of documents via inverted indices),
requires no neural infrastructure, and reliably retrieves documents
containing specific terminology, identifiers, or names. Its limitation is
vocabulary mismatch: if the query asks ``how do I get my money back for a
broken product'' and the relevant document says ``defective merchandise may
be returned for a full refund'', BM25 may rank that document poorly because
the surface vocabularies do not overlap.

\textbf{Semantic retrieval} addresses vocabulary mismatch by representing
queries and documents as dense vectors whose geometry captures meaning.
A specialized embedding model encodes text into a fixed-dimensional vector,
trained so that semantically similar text has similar vectors. Retrieval
finds the documents whose embeddings are most similar to the query embedding,
measured by cosine similarity:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:cosine}
\text{sim}(\mathbf{q}, \mathbf{d}) =
    \frac{\mathbf{q} \cdot \mathbf{d}}{\|\mathbf{q}\| \|\mathbf{d}\|}
\end{equation}

The broken-product query and the defective-merchandise document will have
similar embeddings despite sharing no key words, because the embedding
model has learned that these phrases express the same concept. The cost
is embedding infrastructure: documents must be encoded offline and stored
in a vector database that supports efficient approximate nearest-neighbor
search, and the embedding model must be run at query time.

\textbf{Hybrid retrieval} combines both approaches. BM25 and semantic
search are run in parallel, and their ranked lists are fused using
Reciprocal Rank Fusion (RRF)~\cite{cormack2009reciprocal}:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:rrf}
\text{RRF}(d) = \sum_{r \in R} \frac{1}{k + \text{rank}_r(d)}
\end{equation}

where $R$ is the set of retrieval methods, $\text{rank}_r(d)$ is the rank
of document $d$ in method $r$, and $k = 60$ is a constant preventing
top-ranked documents from dominating. A document ranked first by BM25 and
third by semantic search scores higher than one ranked first by only a
single method. Hybrid retrieval is more robust across query types and
domains than either approach alone, at the cost of maintaining both a
BM25 index and a vector database.

\subsection{Implementation Considerations}
\label{sec:rag_impl}

Several practical decisions significantly affect RAG system quality beyond
the choice of retrieval strategy.

\textbf{Chunking.} Large documents must be split into chunks that fit
within the model's context window. Fixed-size chunks (every $n$ tokens)
are simple but may split mid-sentence. Sentence- or paragraph-based
chunks preserve semantic coherence. Sliding-window chunking with overlap
ensures information near boundaries appears in multiple chunks, preventing
retrieval gaps. Typical production systems use 300--500 token chunks with
10--20\% overlap.

\textbf{Re-ranking.} Initial retrieval may return 20--100 candidates.
A cross-encoder re-ranker then scores each candidate by jointly encoding
the query and the candidate together, producing a more accurate relevance
score than the bi-encoder used for initial retrieval. The top five to ten
re-ranked candidates are included in the prompt. This two-stage pipeline
balances the speed of approximate retrieval with the accuracy of
cross-encoding.

\textbf{Context window management.} Retrieved chunks, the user query, and
space for the model's response must all fit within the context window.
For long contexts, prioritize chunks by re-ranking score, use smaller
chunks, or use models that support longer contexts.

\textbf{When RAG is most valuable.} RAG provides the most benefit when
information changes frequently (news, prices, product details), when
knowledge is proprietary or too large to encode in model parameters, and
when source attribution is required for trust or compliance. RAG is less
suitable for tasks requiring general knowledge well-covered in
pre-training, for creative generation where factual grounding is
unnecessary, or for latency-critical applications where retrieval adds
unacceptable delay.

\section{Supervised Fine-Tuning}
\label{sec:sft}

While prompt engineering and RAG adapt the input to guide a fixed model,
fine-tuning adapts the model itself by updating its parameters through
additional training on task-specific data. This directly encodes task
knowledge into the model's weights, enabling behaviors that cannot be
elicited through prompting alone --- specialized output formats, domain
terminology, stylistic conventions, or reasoning patterns that lie outside
the pre-training distribution.

\subsection{The Fine-Tuning Objective}
\label{sec:sft_objective}

Fine-tuning continues training a pre-trained model on a new dataset
$\mathcal{D}_{\text{task}}$, typically much smaller and more specialized
than the original pre-training corpus. Formally, we seek parameters that
minimize the task loss starting from the pre-trained initialization:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:finetune}
\theta_{\text{ft}} = \arg\min_{\theta}\; \mathcal{L}(\mathcal{D}_{\text{task}};\, \theta),
\quad \theta \text{ initialized from } \theta_{\text{pre}}
\end{equation}

where $\mathcal{L}$ is the cross-entropy loss defined in
Section~\ref{sec:cross-entropy}. Starting from $\theta_{\text{pre}}$ rather
than random initialization is essential: the pre-trained weights encode
general language understanding that would take enormous compute to recover
from scratch.

\subsection{Instruction Fine-Tuning}
\label{sec:instruction_finetuning}

Instruction fine-tuning is a specialized form of supervised fine-tuning
that trains models to follow natural language instructions across a diverse
mixture of tasks. Each training example consists of an instruction
describing the task, optional input context, and the desired output:

\begin{verbatim}
Instruction: Translate the following English text to French.
Input: "Hello, how are you?"
Output: "Bonjour, comment allez-vous?"
\end{verbatim}

The training mixture spans many task types: summarization, question
answering, classification, translation, mathematical reasoning, code
generation, and more. The key finding is that models trained on a
sufficiently diverse mixture of instructions generalize to tasks they
were never explicitly trained on. A model instruction-tuned on translation
and summarization can often perform well on entity extraction despite
never seeing that task, because it has learned to parse and execute
instructions in general rather than memorizing task-specific behaviors.

Instruction-tuned models are dramatically better at zero-shot task
performance than their base counterparts. When a zero-shot prompt fails,
the likely cause is not that the model lacks the underlying capability
but that the base model has not been trained to surface that capability
through instruction following.

\subsection{Data Requirements}
\label{sec:sft_data}

Effective fine-tuning requires data that is sufficient in quantity and
high in quality. As a rough guide, 100--1000 examples can produce modest
improvements on simple tasks; 10,000--100,000 examples are recommended for
robust performance on complex tasks; beyond that, returns depend heavily
on data quality and task difficulty. Importantly, quality dominates
quantity: 1,000 carefully curated examples consistently outperform 10,000
noisy ones. Data should be accurate, consistently formatted, representative
of the inference distribution, and balanced across the task's relevant
dimensions.

When task-specific data is scarce, synthetic data generation --- using a
larger or better model to produce training examples for a smaller one ---
has become a productive strategy. Data augmentation through paraphrasing
or back-translation can expand small datasets. Weak supervision using
heuristics or existing models to generate noisy labels, followed by
careful filtering, enables scaling at lower annotation cost.

\subsection{Hyperparameters and Compute}
\label{sec:sft_hparams}

Fine-tuning uses substantially smaller learning rates than pre-training
--- typically $10^{-5}$ to $10^{-4}$ versus $10^{-4}$ to $10^{-3}$ ---
to avoid overwriting pre-trained knowledge too aggressively. Training
runs for two to ten epochs depending on dataset size, with early stopping
on a held-out validation set. Small weight decay (0.01--0.1) and a linear
or cosine learning rate schedule are standard.

Full fine-tuning of large models is computationally demanding. Models in
the 7--13 billion parameter range can be fine-tuned on a small number of
high-memory accelerators over hours to days. Models with tens to hundreds
of billions of parameters require distributed training across many devices
over days to weeks. Parameter-efficient methods, discussed in the next
section, dramatically reduce these requirements.

\section{Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning}
\label{sec:peft}

Full fine-tuning updates all of a model's parameters, which is both
computationally expensive and, as we discuss in
Section~\ref{sec:forgetting}, risky from the perspective of catastrophic
forgetting. Parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) methods address both
problems by updating only a small subset of parameters --- or adding a
small number of new trainable parameters --- while keeping the vast
majority of the pre-trained weights frozen.

\subsection{The Unifying Principle}
\label{sec:peft_principle}

All PEFT methods share a common hypothesis: \emph{meaningful adaptation
lives in a low-dimensional subspace of the full parameter space}. A
pre-trained model has billions of degrees of freedom, but the change
required to adapt it to a specific task is structurally much simpler ---
it can be approximated by updates that have far fewer independent
dimensions than the full weight matrices.

This hypothesis has strong empirical support. When researchers analyze
the weight changes produced by full fine-tuning, they find that the update
matrices are approximately low-rank: most of the update's ``information
content'' lies in a small number of directions. The pre-trained model
already occupies a good solution in a high-dimensional space; task
adaptation is a small perturbation from that solution, not a wholesale
reorganization.

Different PEFT methods operationalize this hypothesis differently. Adapter
layers insert small bottleneck modules between transformer layers, forcing
adaptation information through a low-dimensional representation. Prefix
tuning and prompt tuning learn a small set of continuous ``virtual tokens''
prepended to the input, constraining adaptation to the space reachable by
modifying those tokens. Sparse fine-tuning identifies and updates only the
small fraction of weights most important for the task. The PEFT landscape
continues to evolve rapidly, and new variants appear regularly. What they
share is more important than what distinguishes them: the hypothesis that
task-relevant change is low-dimensional, and the practical consequence
that freezing most parameters preserves pre-trained knowledge while
enabling meaningful specialization.

LoRA~\cite{hu2021lora} is the most theoretically transparent and
practically dominant instantiation of this principle, and we develop it
in detail as the representative example.

\subsection{LoRA: Low-Rank Adaptation}
\label{sec:lora}

LoRA's core insight is to represent the weight update as a product of two
small matrices whose rank is far lower than the full weight matrix. For a
weight matrix $\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times k}$, instead of
learning an unconstrained update $\Delta\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{R}^{d
\times k}$, LoRA constrains the update to a low-rank form:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:lora}
\mathbf{W}_{\text{ft}} = \mathbf{W}_{\text{pre}} + \Delta\mathbf{W}
    = \mathbf{W}_{\text{pre}} + \mathbf{B}\mathbf{A}
\end{equation}

where $\mathbf{B} \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times r}$ and $\mathbf{A} \in
\mathbb{R}^{r \times k}$ are trainable matrices with rank $r \ll
\min(d, k)$, and $\mathbf{W}_{\text{pre}}$ is frozen throughout training.
Figure~\ref{fig:lora_diagram} illustrates this decomposition.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.80\textwidth]{images/lora_diagram.png}
    \caption{LoRA weight decomposition. The pre-trained weight matrix
    $\mathbf{W}_{\text{pre}}$ (frozen, $d \times k$) is augmented by the
    product of two small trainable matrices $\mathbf{B}$ ($d \times r$)
    and $\mathbf{A}$ ($r \times k$), where rank $r \ll \min(d, k)$.
    The input passes through both paths simultaneously; the outputs are
    summed. At inference, the adapter can be merged into the base weights
    with no computational overhead.}
    \label{fig:lora_diagram}
\end{figure}

The forward pass for input $\mathbf{x}$ computes:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:lora_forward}
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{x}\mathbf{W}_{\text{pre}} + \frac{\alpha}{r}
    \mathbf{x}\mathbf{B}\mathbf{A}
\end{equation}

where $\alpha$ is a scaling constant typically set equal to $r$, making
the effective scaling factor 1. The scaling normalization ensures that
changing the rank $r$ does not require re-tuning other hyperparameters.

\textbf{Initialization.} $\mathbf{A}$ is initialized with a random
Gaussian and $\mathbf{B}$ is initialized to zero, ensuring that
$\Delta\mathbf{W} = \mathbf{B}\mathbf{A} = \mathbf{0}$ at the start
of training. The model therefore begins fine-tuning at exactly the
pre-trained weights and gradually learns the adaptation.

\textbf{Parameter reduction.} The original matrix has $d \times k$
parameters. The LoRA adapter has $r(d + k)$ parameters. When $d \approx
k$, the reduction factor is approximately $\frac{d}{2r}$. For a weight
matrix of size $8192 \times 8192$ with $r = 64$:

\begin{equation}
\text{Original:}\quad 8192^2 = 67{,}108{,}864 \text{ parameters}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\text{LoRA:}\quad 64 \times (8192 + 8192) = 1{,}048{,}576 \text{ parameters}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\text{Reduction:}\quad 64\times \text{ fewer trainable parameters}
\end{equation}

Applied across all attention and feedforward layers of a large model, LoRA
typically reduces trainable parameters by two to three orders of magnitude
--- from tens of billions to tens of millions --- while maintaining
performance close to full fine-tuning.

\textbf{Memory savings.} Because the frozen weights require no gradients
and no optimizer states, the bulk of fine-tuning memory cost is eliminated.
Only the small adapter matrices $\mathbf{B}$ and $\mathbf{A}$ have
associated gradient and optimizer state storage. This enables fine-tuning
models on far fewer accelerators than full fine-tuning would require.

\textbf{Inference.} For deployment with a single adapter, the trained
matrices can be merged into the base weights offline:
$\mathbf{W}_{\text{merged}} = \mathbf{W}_{\text{pre}} + \mathbf{B}
\mathbf{A}$. The merged model is then used for inference with no
computational overhead compared to the original. For deployments serving
many specialized models, the base model can be loaded once and adapter
weights swapped per request, dramatically reducing memory and
infrastructure cost relative to hosting multiple full fine-tuned models.

\textbf{Key hyperparameters.} The rank $r$ is the most important
choice. Values of 4--8 suit simple tasks with limited data; 16--32
work well for most tasks; 64+ approaches the capacity of full
fine-tuning at much lower efficiency. A good default is to start at
$r = 8$ or $r = 16$, increase if the model underfits, and decrease
or add regularization if the validation loss diverges from the
training loss. LoRA is most commonly applied to the query and value
projection matrices in attention layers, though applying it to all
linear layers --- attention and feedforward --- provides more capacity
at the cost of more adapter parameters.

\textbf{Quantization.} Combining LoRA with quantization of the frozen
base weights (storing them in 4-bit rather than 16-bit format) further
reduces memory requirements, enabling fine-tuning of very large models
on modest hardware. The adapter matrices $\mathbf{B}$ and $\mathbf{A}$
are still trained in full precision, preserving update quality despite
the compressed base weights.

\section{Catastrophic Forgetting}
\label{sec:forgetting}

Catastrophic forgetting is the tendency of a neural network to lose
previously learned knowledge when trained on new data. In the context
of fine-tuning, it manifests as degradation of the model's general
capabilities as it specializes for the target task: a model fine-tuned
aggressively on medical text may lose coherent general language
understanding; a model fine-tuned on a narrow classification task may
lose the ability to generate fluent prose.

The mechanism is straightforward. Pre-training finds weights that
perform well across a broad distribution. Fine-tuning optimizes those
same weights for a much narrower distribution. Gradient updates that
improve performance on the fine-tuning data tend to move weights away
from the optimum for the pre-training distribution. With a large enough
learning rate, enough epochs, or a small enough fine-tuning dataset,
the accumulated movement is large enough to erase capabilities that
were encoded in the pre-trained weights.

This is not merely a practical nuisance --- it is the central tension
of the entire adaptation problem. Maximally effective adaptation would
teach the model everything needed for the target task without disturbing
anything else. This is impossible in general, because task-specific and
general knowledge are encoded in the same weights. Every fine-tuning
method is, at some level, a strategy for navigating this trade-off.

\textbf{Why PEFT mitigates forgetting.} The low-rank constraint in LoRA
and similar methods limits how much the effective weights can change.
By freezing the pre-trained weights entirely and only training the
adapter, the vast majority of pre-trained knowledge is structurally
protected --- not because it is regularized toward its original value,
but because it literally does not change. The adapter learns the
task-specific perturbation; the base model retains general knowledge.
This is why LoRA's parameter efficiency and its resistance to
catastrophic forgetting are two sides of the same coin: both follow
directly from the low-rank constraint.

\textbf{Mitigating forgetting in full fine-tuning.} When full
fine-tuning is necessary, several strategies reduce forgetting. Using
a small learning rate ($10^{-5}$ or less) limits the magnitude of each
weight update. Early stopping on a validation set that includes
general-capability tasks, not just the fine-tuning task, catches
degradation before it becomes severe. Including a small fraction of
diverse general-domain data in fine-tuning batches keeps general
capabilities active. L2 regularization toward the pre-trained weights
--- sometimes called elastic weight consolidation --- explicitly
penalizes large deviations from the initialization.

\textbf{The alignment case.} The most consequential instance of the
forgetting problem arises in alignment training, where the goal is to
modify model behavior --- making it safer, more honest, less likely to
cause harm --- while preserving the general capabilities that make it
useful. A model that has been successfully aligned but has lost its
reasoning ability or world knowledge is not a success. The specialized
fine-tuning methods used for alignment, including reinforcement learning
from human feedback, are designed in part to navigate this tension, and
are covered in detail in Chapter~\ref{ch:rlhf}.

\section{Adaptation in Practice}
\label{sec:adaptation_practice}

\subsection{Choosing a Strategy}
\label{sec:adaptation_choice}

The adaptation decision depends on three primary factors: the amount and
quality of available task-specific data, the nature of the gap between
the model's current behavior and the desired behavior, and the available
compute and time.

When task-specific data is absent or very limited, prompt engineering
is the natural starting point. It requires no training, deploys
immediately, and can achieve strong results when the model already
possesses the underlying capability and needs only better guidance. If
performance is insufficient with good prompting, the question is whether
the model lacks the capability entirely or merely needs the right
framing: a few-shot prompt tests the former, and if even excellent
few-shot examples do not produce adequate outputs, fine-tuning is likely
necessary.

When task-specific data is available in the thousands to hundreds of
thousands of examples, parameter-efficient fine-tuning is almost always
the right choice. It provides substantially better performance than
prompting on tasks that require specialized behavior, at dramatically
lower compute cost than full fine-tuning, and with meaningful protection
against catastrophic forgetting. Full fine-tuning is warranted when
the task is radically different from the pre-training distribution in
ways that low-rank updates cannot capture, or when compute is not a
constraint and maximum performance on the specific task is paramount.

When information changes frequently --- prices, news, product details,
user-specific context --- RAG provides capabilities that fine-tuning
cannot: access to data that did not exist at training time. Fine-tuning
encodes knowledge into weights, which cannot be updated at query time;
RAG retrieves knowledge from a database that can be updated continuously.
These are complementary, not competing: a fine-tuned model that has
internalized domain language and task format, combined with RAG that
injects current factual information, often outperforms either approach
alone.

Figure~\ref{fig:adaptation_decision} summarizes this decision logic.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{images/adaptation_decision.png}
